Special- Dispatch to The Call.
READING. Pa., May 12.â A collision
of passenger trains occurred on the
Philadelphia and Reading Railroad at
Exeter, about six miles south of this
city, to-night, and a great number of
pet-pie were killed nr injured. There is
no telegraph office at Exeter and de
tails, therefore, are difficult to obtain.
The number of killed is variously stated
to be from fifteen to twenty-five. Fully
fifty others are injured.
The regular express train from Potts
ville for Philadelphia connected at th"
station in Reading with a train from
Harrisburg. which was â¢â - -led with
excursionists., who had been to the
State? capital to witness the ceremonies
In connection with the unveiling of the
Hartranft monument. Many of the
Harrisburg passengers at Reading went
aboard the Philadelphia express, but.
It b^ing found that all could not be ac
commodated, it was decided to send an
extra train tb Philadelphia to run as
the second section of the express. The
extra train left twenty minutes later
than the' express. At Exeter the ex
press stopped for orders, and while
standing still the extra train crashed
Into it while moving at great speed.
Three of the rear cars of the express
â were telescoped and the first car of
the extra train was wrecked. The pas,
senders in these cars were terribly
mangled, many being killed outright,
while other? had limbs and bodies
maimed. Word was at once sent to
Reading and a special train with phy- j
sicians and nurses was sent to th I
ecene as quickly as it could possibly be
put into readiness. The identified billed j

The San Francisco Call.
ROSWELL P. FLOWER
IS CALLED TO REST
NEW YORK, May 12. -Former Governor Roswell P. Flower died to
night at 10:30 at the Eastport Country Club at Eastport, L. I. Mr.
Flower was taken ill early in the day with a severe attack of acute in
. digestion. In the afternoon symptoms of heart failure supervened and
he grew steadily worse until the time of his death. The attack of
heart failure was accompanied by a fainting spell, and Mr. Flower's fam
ily in New York City was quickly notified. Mrs. Flower and the former
Governor's nephew, Frederick S. Flower, accompanied by Dr. Thomas H.
Allen. Mr. Flower's physician, left at once on a special train for Eastport.
When they arrived there Mr. Flower had somewhat recovered, but to-night
the attack of heart failure was marked and Mr. Flower became unconscious
an hour or more before his death.
Mr. Flower had been a sufferer from gastritis for a long time, every
now and then an acute attack. For a month or two past he has been a
regular visitor at the Eastport Country Club in the hope that he would find
some relief in the outing He appeared in robust health yesterday, and this
morning when he arrived at stport, where he intended to remain until
Monday, his health was apparently excellent. The attack from which he suf
fered to-day came on suddenly and was of a more severe type at the outset
than any he had previously suffered from.
Frederick S. Flower took charge of the details after his uncle's death,
but no arrangement for the funeral has yet been made. The body will prob
ably be transferred to Manhattan to-morrow morning.
Rosweil P. Flower was born in Theresa, Jefferson County, N. V., Au
gust 7. 1835. Roswell was the sixth of nine children and when his father
died he was 8 years old. He worked with his brothers on two farms his
mother owned and spent his time between school sessions in the hardest
kind of labor. He became deputy postmaster of Watertown, N. V., in 1853
and kept his place six years.' Then he became a partner in the jewelry
firm of Hitchcock & Flower, bought out his partner at the end of two
years and continued in the business until 1869.
Mr. Flower came to New York in that year to take charge of the estate
of Henry Keep, whose widow was Mrs. Flower's sister. Mr. Keep owned a
great many railroad and other stocks and during the months before his
death spent much time in instructing Mr. Flower about them. Having
thus gained an insight into Wall street and its methods Mr. Flower formed
in 1Â«70 the firm of Benedict. Flower & Co., in which he joined E. C. Benedict
and H. H. Truman. This firm was dissolved in I^7_. Not long afterward he
organized the banking firm of Flower & Co., which has since been a power
in Wall street.
Mr. Flower always was a Democrat. In the early seventies he helped
Samuel J. Tilden to develop the famous organization which exposed Tweed
and enjoyed many other triumphs. Mr. Flower was chosen chairman of
the Democratic State Committee in 1877, and in 1881 he defeated William
Waldorf Astor for Congress after* a hot campaign in the Eleventh District.
His majority was 3100, while Levi P. Morton, Republican, had carried it at
the last previous election by more than 7000 votes. He refused a renomin
ation. He was elected Governor of the State in 1891J
Mr. Flower took up Brooklyn Rapid Transit less than two years ago
buying from 20.000 to 30,000 shares at about $25, when the capital stock was
only $25,000,000. He absorbed many subordinate lines, among them the
Nassau Electric Company, and increased the capital stock to $45,000,000. and
by his extraordinarily successful manipulation, put the price up to 137 His
holdings in Brooklyn Rapid Transit alone, which cost him two years ago
little more than $500,000. ire worth, at to-day's market price more than
$3,000,000. , . *

POTTER SAYS
BRIGGS WILL
BE ORDAINED
NEW YORK, May Bishop Potter
gave out the following formal notice
â  to-day:
"The Rev. Dr. Briggs and the Rev.
Charles H. Snedecker will be ordained
on the day officially appointed by the
Bishop of New York, May 14, at the
pro-cathedral, Stanton street. New-
York, at 10:45 a. m. The Rev. George
William Douglass will preach the ordi
! nation sermon. The Rev. Charles B.
Smith will present Dr. Briggs and the
Rev. George Francis Nelson will pre
sent Mr. Snede<
Bishop Potter chose the pro-cathedral
; for the place because Dr. Briggs had
asked to be assigned to work there and
: will work there in future.
'The Churchman of this week says:
The sensational stories that have ap
peared in the daily press with regard to
the ordination of Dr. Briggs suggested the
necessity of giving to our readers certain
Information -that may help them to a
right judgment in the premises.
Rev. Dr. Briggs was ordained to the
dlaconate last year, having met all
canonical regulations, and after having
been recommended by a standing com
mittee, composed of the Revs. Morgan
Dix. Thomas H. Harris, Octavus Apple
gate and J. S. Shlpman and Messrs.
Stephen P. Nash. G. A. Miller. S. Nichol
son Kane and Herman C. Van Post. He
has met all the requirements for advance
ment to the prieshood and has been rec
ommended again by this same committee,
with only one change in Its composition.
Many persons have been disturbed by
passages quoted in the dally papers from
Dr. Briggs' late book. It is only fair to
state that most' of these passages appear
to be wrested from their true setting.

CATHOLIC CITIZENS OF
'> THE UNITED STATES
Monsignor Martinelli on the Attitude
of the Church Regarding Re
publican Governments.
NOTRE DAME, Ind.. May -Monsig
nor Martinelli to-night gave out the fol
lowing interview:
I am delighted with the news and the evi
dence that our Catholic citizen* are among
the most ardent supporters of the American
Government. In some quarters there is an er
roneous impression that the papal church is
opposed to a republican form of government.
It Is not necessary to leave the Western hem
isphere to prove the untruth of th".? assertion.
Most of the countries in South America are
republics, and in all of them a vast majority
of the citizens are Catholics. . ,r ,
In the matter of education. I think science,
religion and knowledge should go hand ln hand.
Revealed religion' especially should have a
place In the schools. In the matter of moral
ity America ranks with the foremost nations
of the world. Concerning the growth of the
Catholic religion in America I can say It Is
making a greater number of converts now than
at any previous time, especially among the
higher classes. . '.''c ' . - '
The Pope's attitude toward the question of
the war has been emphasized In no doubtful
way. He is emphatically for peace. He Is
willing to arbitrate in the settlement of any
differences when his services are requested:
when they are not. he indirectly uses his In
fluence as best he can.
I am delighted that the Catholic Institutions
of America are keeping abreast of the times. i
The church has always been a patron of art
and science. Concerning Notre Dame, I- have j
seen nothing like It among the colleges I have
visited In this country-
THE SYRIAN REPUBLIC
NOT TAKEN SERIOUSLY
Declaration of Independence Looked
Upon Only as a Symptom of
Unrest.
WASHINGTON, May 12.â manifes
to declaring the independence of Syria, is
sued yesterday in New York by the Young
Syria Junta, is not taken seriously in of
ficial quarters here except as an evidence
of the unrest which has prevailed among
Turkish subjects living abroad, Includ
ing the Syrians, Armenians and the Turks
themselves, who conduct a revolutionary
organization known as Young Turks.
The reports from United States officers
in Syria and from United States Minister
Straus at Constantinople have shown no
recent uprising or discontent in Syria.
The Turkish Minister. All Ferroupbey,
said to-day the manifesto was entirely
without importance and did not represent
a sentiment prevailing among the Syrians
in this country or at home. Only recent
ly the Minister was given a handsome re
ception In New York by the resident Sy
rians, during which the most cordial man
ifestations were shown him and this Is
said to be the sentiment among the seri
ous and responsible element of this peo
ple. The only opposition element, the
Minister points out. Is made up of a few
men interested in publishing Arabic pa
pers in New York and other parts of the
country, but these, he says, in no way
voice the sentiments of the Syrians.

Was Going to Kill Himself and Was
Loth to Leave His Wife
Behind.
HOWARD CITY. Mich.. May 12. o
seph Harvey, murderer' of his grand
mother, uncle and wife, who also fatally

Jail at Stanton to-day. The young man
slept soundly all night, and when ques

expressed no emotion over, his atrocious
crime. He has given no indications
whatever of Insanity.
He says his uncle, Pierson, quarreled
with him and abused him because he had
driven Pierson's horse in' the rain. He
still insists his- dead, uncle killed' his
grandmother, and he says he then killed
his uncle out of revenge. He says he is
sorry he killed his wife, -but he did it be
cause he intended to kill himself, and
did not want her. to live after he was
gone. '" " ' ' ' ----- - <>

vey was not known to have any vicious

STATUE OF GENERAL
HARTRANFT UNVEILED
HARRISBURG, Pa., May 12.â The bronze equestrian statue of Gen
eral John F. Hartranft in Capitol Park was unveiled with impres
sive ceremonies this afternoon in the presence of a large concourse.
After the ceremonies in Capitol Park a procession formed with
General Levi A- Dodd of Baltimore as chief marshal and J. P. Cooke
of Lewiston as chief of staff. The reviewing party included Governor and
Mrs. Stone, Mrs. Hartranft and family. Secretary Alger, Postmaster Gen
eral Smith, Adjutant General Corbin, Mrs. Levi A. Dodd of Baltimore,
Major General Gregg of Reading and members of the Senate and House
of Representatives. There were over 3000 men in line. The survivors of
the Third Division of the Ninth Army Corps, commanded by General Hart
ranft in the Civil War, had the right of line. The procession also in
cluded the Ninth Regiment of immune? from Camp Meade, a provisional
brigade of the National Guard, the Grand Army of the Republic posts and
Sons of Veterans camps of Harrisburg and vicinity and the Harrisburg
fire department.
The statue represents General Hartranft as he appeared at the close of
the war. He is shown returning to his native town in the act of saluting
the people, who applaud him and throw across his path laurel and palm
branches, over which the horse walks as if he felt proud to carry his mas
ter on the occasion of his triumphal return home. The statue is nearly
fourteen feet high, and is mounted on a fourteen-foot polished red granite
pedestal in front of the Capitol of Pennsylvania, at Harrisburg.
John Frederick Hartranft was born at New Hanover, Montgomery
County, Pa., on December 16, 1830. During the Civil War he was a colonel
of Pennsylvania volunteers and won his stars by carrying the famous
stone bridge at Antietam. He was appointed major general, and command
ed a division in General Burnside's Ninth Army Corps. General Hartranft
was Governor of Pennsylvania from 1873 to 1879 and in 18-76 was Pennsyl
vania's candidate for the Presidency.
. After the expiration of his term as Governor, General Hartranft devoted
his life to the reorganization of the Pennsylvania National Guard, of which
he was made major general. It was through his military-knowledge and
ability that it became the model guard of the country. Major General Hart
ranft died at Norristown, Pa., on October 17, 18S9.
AGUIRRE IS
NOT YET IN
HALES PLACE
SAN QUENTIN PRISON, May 12.â '
When the Board of State Prison Di
rectors met in regular monthly session
to-day it was confidently predicted that
the sun would rise to-morrow morning,
and cast its beams on the head of a new
Warden. The wiseacres were sure of
this because somebody had said Gov
ernor Gage was to be present at the
meeting by his booted and august per
sonality. Gage was at the meeting, and
so was ex-Sheriff Martin G. Aguirre.
but W. E. Hale retains the wardenship.
The Governor arrived, in company |
with Aguirre and Attorney Chretien,
on the noon train, and. together with
the Directors, dined with the Warden.
Afterward the party was escorted
throughout the prison and initiated into
the mysteries of the jute mill. Several
prisoners from the south were recog
nized by Gage, who shook hands with
them and in two Instances stepped
away from the party to hold private
conferences with convicts. One, Cap
tain James, who is serving three years
for having sent obscene literature
through the mails, was made the espe
cial recipient of Gage's favors, and af
ter the hearing of a private grievance
Warden Hale was beckoned to join
the two.
The board went into executive ses
sion at 2 o'clock, and was closeted with
the Governor for nearly three hours.
It was afterward announced that
Aguirre had not been elected Warden,
for very good reasons.
"Mr. Aguirre will be appointed War
den at the meeting to be held at Fol
som a week from to-morrow, or at a
special meeting a few days later," said
Director Hayes. "At this session he
was simply introduced to us by the
Governor, who spoke highly of his
qualifications and said it was his de
sire to see him elected. Mr. Aguirre
has proved a pleasing candidate to tha
board, and will be the next Warden un
less something unforeseen occurs."
The Governor, with Aguirre, Chretien
and several others, was taken to San
Francisco on the stern-wheeler Caro
line as soon as the meeting adjourned..
All appeared in good humor, and both
Gage and his protege wore smiles of
satisfaction. V.'- "..
At the morning session Warden Hale
read his report on the punishment he
thought merited the nine conspirators
who tried to escape from the prison.
It was adopted in the afternoon, when â 
Director Devlin arrived, making a full
board. :^i .;'
George W. Schlegal, George Bullock.
Edward Morrell and H. W. Cooper were
termed ringleaders, and in addition to
the punishment which they have al
ready received, the first three, being
"lifers," are to be confined in the soli
tary cells in the front .cell building un- |
til the incorrigible cells are completed.
They will then be confined in the incor
rigible cells and forced to do hard la
bor in the manufacture of grainbags
by hand at the discretion of the War
den. Cooper will lose all his credits,
besides receiving the same punishment :
as the other ringleaders. Si Loverin, !
H. M. Shaw and James Roup will be ;
confined in the Incorrigible department â¢
at the discretion of the Warden. Alton I
H. Gould has already forfeited all cred
its and is now confined in the prison
hospital with rheumatism. Punishment
in his case is suspended until such time
as the administering of it may be
deemed advisable. As - only eight
months' credits can be forfeited by
Cooper, King and Chevalier will lose
their credits for the same time, as it
was deemed only just to make them
lose no more than a ringleader. All the
conspirators are to be kept dressed in
red flannel shirts.
Walter Furnish and Louis Ferez
pleaded guilty to an attempt at smug
gling opium into the prison on May 8
and were deprived of all their credits.
Their cases are to be referred to Dis
trict Attorney Mclsaac, with instruc
tions to prosecute both men as soon as
their terms expire in autumn. These
are the first opium smugglers detected
since the law making smuggling a fel
ony went into effect, and an example
will be made of them.
The Warden's grainbag and Jute re
ports were about the same as last
month. His financial report shows that
the sale of grainbags will leave $160,000
for the State prison fund after the jute
revolving fund is filled.
Woman Burned to Death.
LOS ANGELES, May 12. â Mrs. A.
Moore, employed as housekeeper by Hen
ry Kagel, a bee-keeper, residing near San
Fernando, was burned to death early
this morning In a. fire which also com
pletely destroyed the Kagel residence.
The cause of the fire is not at present
known and will remain a mystery until
an investigation has been made by the
Coroner. Th* building and contents were
valued at $500.

MANILA, May 12.â The forces of
Major General Mac-Arthur and Major
General Lawton are being; strength
ened. The insurgent troops are strongly
intrenched on both fronts.
Fresh troops are beginning to go to
the front. Two battalions of the Seven
teenth Infantry (regulars) that had
been holding the lines about the city of
Manila will join General Mac Arthur's
division at San Fernando to-morrow,
and one battalion of the same regiment
will re-enforce Major General Lawton's
division near Bacolor. These troops
will be replaced here by the Twenty
first Infantry Regiment, which arrived
from the United States on the transport
Hancock yesterday.
Captain Grant of the Utah Battery,
whose success in managing, the gun
boats Laguna de Bai and Cavadonga
has won for him the sobriquet of "The
Dewey of the Army.'' has been put in
command of the recently purchased
Spanish gunboats, the arms of which
the insurgents captured. These ves
sels are now being prepared for opera
tions on the rivers and along the coast.
The arming of the boats is being
pushed with all diligence.
FILIPINO CONGRESS
EVENLY DIVIDED
LONDON. May 12.â A special dispatch
received here from Manila says that the
Filipino Congress, now sitting at San
Isidro. is composed of fifty-six members,
of whom twenty favor peace and an equal
number are irreconcilables. The others,
holding the balance of power, are ready
to admit that absolute independence is
beyond attainment, but they demand bet
ter terms at the hands of the United
States.

DAMAGED BY THE
ILOILO BOMBARDMENT
LONDON. May 12.-In the House of
Commons to-day the Right Hon. St. John
Broderick, Parliamentary Secretary of
Foreign Affairs, replying to a question by
John St. Clair, Radical member for For
farshire, relative to destruction of British
property by the bombardment of Hollo,
said that the Government had no Infor
mation with regard to the intention of
the United States to compensate traders.
The question of these claims, the speaker
said was under consideration by the
British Government and will not be lost
sight of. â __
YUKON RIVER IS NOW
OPEN TO NAVIGATION
Steamer Flora Plows Her Way
Through the Floating Ice
to Dawson.
VICTORIA, May 12.â Navigation has
opened on the Yukon. The river steamer
Flora of the Lake Bennett and Klondike
Navigation Company was the first vessel
to plow her way through the floating ice
between Dawson City and Lake Lebarge.
According to news received by the steamer
Amur, which arrived this evening, the
Flora was launched at the foot of Lake
Lebarge on April 15, and two days later
she headed for Dawson City. Her prow
had been covered with steel pistes to
protect her from the floating Ice packs.
The trip was necessarily slow, lor in
places so thick was the Ice that she had
practically to cut her way through. The
vessel managed to reach the Klondike
capital after fourteen days of perilous
navigation. Site was received there with
great eclat, and after spending but one
brief day at the capital her steel-sheathed
prow" was headed up the river to fight
her way back through the drifting ice.
News or her safe arrival at Lake Lebarge
was received at Skaguay before the
Amur started. Her passengers may be
looked for on the next downward steam-
ers.
The White Pass and Chilcoot trails are
now in very bad condition and packers
have been compelled to suspend opera
â tions. J. Haney and Alexander Banner
man of this city, who have been working
big pack trains on the Atiin trails, re
turned with their horses on the Amur.
A miner named McMillan died at the
Teepee Hotel on May 4 while en route to
FOUR MEN KILLED
AND FIVE MISSING
Fatal Collapse of a Big Heap of Coal
Refuse at Shamokin, Penn
sylvania.
SHAMOKIN. Pa., May 12.â Four men
were instantly killed to-day by a slide of
a portion of a large coal bank at Cen
tralis. Five others are missing, and are
probably dead. Several were badly in
jured. The four bodies recovered were so
badly disfigured that it was difficult to
identify them.
The accident occurred -while a large
number of Hungarians were screening
coal in the Centralis breaker. The big
bank of coal refuse which towered high
above was seen to quiver and collapse,
completely burying the men A rescuing
force immediately started to work and
succeeded In recovering the four bodies.
Two Hungarians were found wedged
in between piles of timber. They were
badly injured.
JAMES FREEMAN'S SUICIDE.
Atlin Tragedy the Result of a Family
Quarrel.
SEATTLE, May 12.â The steamer Faral
lon, which arrived to-night, brought the
details of the suicide of James Freeman
of California at Atlin on May 3. Some
months ago he had trouble with his wife
and went to" Skaguay. Later he returned
to California and tried to effect a compro
mise, but was unsuccessful. He returned
to Skaguay and she followed. Later he
went to Atlin, but being out of money and
unable to obtain work he became discour
aged and committed suicide. The Cana
dian police took charge of the body, but
later the widow appeared on the scene and
asserted her rights-
NOOSE FOR THE
MURDERER OF LINN
Claude Branton Hanged
at Eugene.
â â¦ â
EUGENE, Or.. May ' Claude
Branton was hanged to-day for the
murder of John Linn. Branton left his
cell at 10:45. and walking upon the I
scaffold unassisted he took his place j
I upon the trap. He asked the people j
present not to bear any ill will against j
his relatives on his account. The trap j
'. was sprung at 10:50. Fifteen minutes I
later the body was cut down.
The crime for which Claude Branton j
paid the penalty of death was the tak- |
' ing of the life of John Linn, a stock- ;
man of Condon, Gilliam County. In
April, IS9B, Branton and Courtland !
Green, who is now serving a life sen
tence in the penitentiary for complic
ity in the crime, left their, homes near
Walterville, Lane County, to go to
Eastern Oregon to work. Branton had
received from Linn a proposition to
' work for him herding a band of horses.
While working in Eastern Oregon
Branton and Green laid plans to mur
der Linn to get the horses and some
money which they believed he had.
They induced him to cross the moun
tains with them, representing to him
that on arriving at Branton's home at
Walterville he (Branton) would trade
â¢ him a piece of land for an interest in
: the horses. Before starting he was
told that Branton and Green had
planned to get away with him. but he
I did not believe it.
On the night of June IV Linn rolled
; himself in his blankets near the camp
fire and went to sleep. After he had
| been asleep for some time Branton shot
him two or three times with a revol
ver and then, assisted by Green, placed
I the body on the/ fire. They remained
up all night and frequently replenished
the fire with rails until the body was-,
consumed. Then they raked the small
â¢ pieces of bones from the ashes, tied
them in a sack and hid them.
Branton left the country, but news
,of the crime soon got out. On July 3
Courtland Green, who was arrested,
made a confession, and a search was
; immediately commenced for Branton.
In the latter part of July he returned
to Eugene and was arrested before he
knew that the story of the crime had
been made public.

The second relief train left Exeter at
2:50 a. m. It brought to Reading twenty
additional bodies. The names are not
yet known. Most of the seriously in
jured were brought here on the first
train. The rest of the Injured were
taken to Pottstown and Norristown.
The northbound track has been cleared
and the first train through to this city
from Philadelphia was the Buffalo ex
press, which left Philadelphia at 11:36
p. m.
PHILADELPHIA, May 12. â The
Pottsville express, which was run into
at Exeter, arrived here fb-night with
a few of the injured. They were:
George W. Cooper. Common Council
man of the Twentieth Ward, this city,
injured about . the legs; Francis T.
Steinbeck, Camden, N. J., cut about the
head and bruised about the body; C. A.
Beaver, Philadelphia, arm bruised and
head cut; B. Barney, Philadelphia, leg.
arm and hip badly bruised.
The passengers could give no con
nected story of the wreck and the num
ber of dead and injured was not known
by any of them. The railroad officials
in this city can give no details of the
accident. They say they are ignorant
of the number of casualties.
United States Senator Penrose and
General F. B. Grubb were passengers
in the Pullman car of the wrecked
train. The rear end of the car in which
they were riding was crushed and the
two gentlemen were badly shaken. Sen
ator Penrose did not know how many
of the passengers of the rear cars were
killed or injured, but said when the
train left the scene those needing at
j tention were being cared for by phy
sicians and residents of the neighbor
! hood. He learned that several were
[ killed and many badly Injured, but as
;to the number he could not say. A few
lof those whose Injuries were, slight
came on the train to Philadelphia, but
j the train left soon after the accident
i and the more seriously injured were left
, at the scene and sent to Reading.
TRAIN COLLISION IN
UTICA RAILWAY YARDS
UTICA, N. V., May 13.â At 2:35 o'clock
this morning New York Central train
No. 33, the Southwestern limited, while
In the yard In this city, was run into
by Pan-American express train No. 31,
resulting in the destruction of a Wag
ner Car, an express car and the engine
of the latter train. The wreck caught
fire, but the flames were extinguished
by the local department. Harry Neal
of Buffalo, assistant superintendent of
the Wagner Company, was so badly
hurt that he may die. He is at a hos
pital. No one else was hurt.

Special Dispatch to The Call.
PINOLE, May. 12.â James M. Clancy
shot and killed Henry Welsh at Ro
land's blacksmith ship on San Pablo
Creek last night at 10 o'clock. Both
men had been drinking very heavily
all the evening. They became engaged
in an altercation over some trivial mat
ter. Welsh became very much excited
and drew a knife on Clancy, who tried
to escape from the building, but Welsh
jumped into the doorway and prevented
his retreat. Clancy then drew his re
volver and shot Welsh four times, kill
ing him instantly.
Both men are quite young and were
born and raised in this vicinity.
Word of the murder was brought to
the Sheriff this morning about 3 o'clock
and officers immediately started for
Orinda.
Clancy gave himself up. He claims
that the shooting was done in self-de
fense.
Roland says he was awakened by
! some one knocking on the building; he
; called out and Welsh walked in and
asked for money with which to pur
chase some wine. The old roan in
formed him that he had no money, but
gave him an order.
In the meantime Clancy had come in
I and the three then sat down to the ta
: ble, when Welsh returned and com*
j menced to drink. Just how the row
, started is not known, but the old man \
\ says that as the boys kept drinking
they became angry, and that finally
Welsh said to Clancy: "I gave your
i brother a good licking a short time ago
and I can do you up just as good."
Welsh then grabbed a butcher knife
that was on the table and made a lunge
cat Clancy. Clancy dodged the blow,
i but Welsh followed him up, and just j
i as Welsh was about to plunge the knlfa
j into Clancy, Clancy pulled his revolver
| and fired.
Clancy is said to be the son of a
I wealthy rancher.

A dead MAN'S grave
Bishop Montgomery Brings Action
Against the William McDer
mott Estate.
LOS ANGELES, May 12â Bishop Mont
gomery, head of the Catholic church in
Southern California, desires payment of
claim 136 due the Catholic church of this
city from the estate of William McDer
mott, who was proprietor of the Fashion
livery stable and died a year ago, leav
ing an estate valued at . $5000. Bishop
Montgomery's claim is for a dead man's
grave in the Catholic cemetery.
Mrs. Carrie McDermott, special admin
istratrix, filed a supplementary account
ing of receipts and disbursements. The
account snows that the estate, which one
year ago was valued at $5000, now has a
balance of $32 to its credit. The widow
says the funds have been handled by At
torney Albert J. Sherer. Of the $75 month
ly allowance awarded by the Probate
Court she says she received barely $200 in
a year. She testified that she could ob
tain from the estate barely enough to live
on.
A short time ago the livery stable was
sold for $1900. Of this sum the $32 re
ferred to now remains. Mrs. McDermott
says Sherer, who handled the funds, told
her that the money went ior the deDts
and expenses of the livery stable.
Attorney Zach Montgomery, represent
ing Bishop Montgomery, this morning filed
In the Superior Court a sensational at
tack upon the report of the special admin
istrator. Bishop Montgomery's objections
to the account are based on the grounds
first, that the money alleged to have been
paid out on various items was never paid
at allâ that the account is false.
Mrs. Mary E. Murdock, sister of the de
ceased. In the spring of 1898 filed a con
test to the probate of William McDer
mott's will, alleging that Mrs. Carrie Mc-
Dermott was not in fact the legal wife
of William McDermott. She alleged that
at the time of her marriage to McDer
mott she had another husband living.
This contest Is yet to be heard.
DEFAULTER CROSBY IS
CAUGHT IN WYOMING
BAKERSFIELD, May 12. -Sheriff Borg
wardt has received a message from Raw
lins, Wyo., stating that the authorities
there have captured J. M. Crosby, who
is wanted here. Crosby was a drummer
for Hooker & Co. of San Francisco. On
one of his visits here he collected $500
from Stoner Brothers for his firm and
then disappeared. This was in September,
1898. He. gave a receipt for the money.
When captured he was passing under the
name of J. O. Connell. Sheriff Borgwardt
will go after him.

CENSURE FOR
MAJOR CUIGNET
Divulged Secrets to the
Petit Journal.
WERE IN OFFICIAL LETTERS
â Â« â
EXCITING SCENES IN THE CABI
NET AT PARIS,
*
The Affair Ended With an Over
whelming Vote Expressing
Confidence in the Gov
ernment.
â¢
Special Dispatch to The Call.
PARIS, May 12.â At a sitting of the
Cabinet Council in the palace of the
Elysee to-day. the President, M. Loubet,
on the suggestion of the Minister of War,
M. Camllle Krantz, signed a decree cen
suring Major Cuignet on account of his
having divulged to the Petit Journal the
contents of certain letters.
In the Chamber of Deputies to-day M.
Rene Ylvianl, Radical Socialist, ques
tioned the Government regarding the let
ters published in the Petit Journal. In
reply M. Krantz said M. Cuignet came
voluntarily to him and confessed he
gave the letters to a friend, who had
communicated them to the Petit Journal.
M. del Casse, Minister of Foreign Af
fairs, said the letters as published were
garbled and mutilated. Among mingled
cheers and hisses, M. del Casse explained
his own position in the matter, and as
serted that Major Cuignet, as a member
of the War Office, had impugned the good
faith of the Foreign Office in certain mat
ters. He (M. del Casse) therefore wrote
to M. de Freycinet. the then Minister of
War, asking apologies for the War Of
fice and showing that Major Cuignet's ac
cusations were unfounded.
The impression gained from M. del
Casse's speech was that M. de Freycinet
resigned the war portfolio instead of
making reparation. The discussion in the
Chamber ended with a vote of confidence
in the Governmentâ 3B9 to 64. - V
In the course of his speech M.
del Casse declared that in reply
to his demand for reparation Major
Cuignet wrote regretting the publicity
given his statement, but declining to
withdraw It. M. del Casse then demanded
that translations of the Panzardissl tele
grama should be submitted to the Court
of Cassation in order that that tribunal
might determine which was correct. M.
del Casse protested against the charg3
of having dictated M. Paleologue's evi
dence and said he had only desired that
M. Pairologue prove to the court the good
faith of the Foreign Office. The trans
lation made for the Foreign Office was
covi'terslgned by M. Chanoine, Major
Cuignet and M. ' Paieologue.
It was this statement, M. del Casse con
tinued, which the Foreign Office sent to
the Ministry of War, and therefore Major
Cuignet's charge was a gratuitous false
hood, as he had himself admitted the cor
rectness of the Foreign Office's transla
tion by countersigning it.
The Nationalist Deputy Lasiez has sent
seconds to M. del Casse to demand ex
planations for the hitter's statements in
the Chamber of Deputies yesterday with
reference to M. Lasiez's attacks, to the
effect that he spurned them with his toes,
although that was more than they de
served. M. del Casse informed M. Lasiez's
seconds that he had nothing to add to
his previous remarks, and that he de
clined to appoint seconds.
M. Gross jean, a Judge of the Versailles
court and an intimate friend of Major
Cuignet, has been cited to appear before
the Superior Council of the magistracy
to-morrow to explain his participation in
the divulging of letters published in the
Petit Journal.
The Paris correspondent of the Daily
Chronicle says: Sensational announce
ments regarding the preparations for the
return of Dreyfus to France toward the
end of next month continue; but the story
that ten members of the Republican
Guard and four gendarmes left Saint
Nazare on Tuesday for French Guiana to
escort him -back lacks authentication as
yet.
WARDNER PRISONERS
ARE BADLY TREATED
SALT LAKE, Utah, May 12.â The West
ern Labor Union and the Federation of
Western Miners held meetings to-day at
10 a. m. and 2 p. m. No business of public
Importance was transacted. The labor
union is still working on constitution and
by-laws and Is not nearly through with
this subject.
In the miners' meeting the committee
on by-laws completed its report, which
was adopted. The committee on griev
ances recommended changes in the card
system so that the back of the card
should show how much each holder had
paid to his union; that is, it should Indi
cate his standing.
Attorney Walter A. Jones Is here to
consult with the miners' federation con
cerning the defense of the 800 or 1000
miners under arrest in the Coeur d'Alene
mining district, charged with various
crimes, from arson to murder. He says
the condition of the prisoners is deplor
able. .There were fifteen boxcars full of
men on a sidetrack last Saturday night
when he left, and 400 or more were hud
dled in a barn.
WALLACE, Idaho. May 12.â County
Commissioners Boyle and Stimson,
charged with complicity In the recent
riots, have applied for a writ of habeas
corpus and Judge Mayhew will hear ar
guments Monday. Many have expressed
the opinion that without a declaration of
martial law by the President, the county
officers continue in pow r er. This case is
brought to settle the point. To-morrow
an order of Judge Mayhew for a special
term of the District Court to convene on
May 29 will be published. Impeachment
proceedings against the county officers
will come up first and the cases against
the alleged rioters later.

OUR ISSUES
WITH CANADA
Prospect of a Treaty to
Settle Matters.
â â¦ â
LONDON, May 12.â With reference
to negotiations by the Washington
joint commission, the correspondent
here of the Associated Press has ob
tained from the highest quarters the
following authoritative statement:
"Negotiations have never been broken
off nor endangered at any time since
the adjournment of the commission. If
matters proceed as smoothly as they
are at present proceeding here Sir
Julian Pauncefote's return to Washing
ton will be signalized by the conclusion
of a treaty embracing every question
in dispute in a manner honorable and
pleasing to the two countries."
Due notice has been taken by the
officials here of the semi-official declar
ation from London respecting the
chances for the conclusion of a treaty
that will settle all of the questions at
issue 'between the United States and
Canada. All that can be said is that
the administration will be extremely
gratified if Sir Julian Pauncefote re
turns to the United States with instruc
tions which will meet the difficulties
which baffled the first committee, for,
as matters stand, it is realized that
nothing can be expected to result from
further meeting of the commission.
The statement that negotiations are
to be deferred until the return of Sir
Julian Pauncefote to Washington is re
garded as significant. It confirms fully
the original statement that the com
mission cannot meet until August next,
but the Embassador's return to this
country was set for autumn.
The dispatch also appears to convey
an intimation that the British Govern
ment has about reached the conclusion
that the only method by which these
vexatious issues can be adjusted is by
direct negotiations between the repre
sentatives of the governments of Great
Britain add the United States.
The parties to the negotiations up to
this point have expressed perfect con
fidence in their ability to reach a satis
factory agreement between themselves
in a very short period of time if allowed
to do what they believe right, so they
will be glad to be relieved of the inter
vening forces. "â ;â  'â â :., 'â -â

BE RAISED FOR DEWEY
NEW YORK, May 12.â New
York Times in an editorial entitled
"What Shall We Do for Dewey?" to
morrow will say:
What shall we do for the man who has
dons much for up That Is the question
which has for a long time been agitating
the American people. It Is quite true
that, according to our precedents, we
have not been niggardly In recognition
of the great services of "Commodore"
Dewey, as he was a year ago. But there i
is a persistent and prevailing feeling that
we ought to do more; that the Ameri
can nation in some unofficial and sponta
neous way should recognize the services
of Dewey as the British nation recognized

say. Evidently it will not do to make up :

mercy of a group of millionaires, Evl- j
dently it will not do to exclude from the
national testimonial to the admiral any
American citizen who desires to bear his
part In it. Why not appeal directly to
the public through its immediate organs
and agents, the newspapers, which get
their living by representing Its senti
ments?
There are some 20,000 periodicals in the i
United States which are entitled to trans
mission as such through the malls.
Among them are 2119 daily papers, which
represent most immediately and most ac
curately the sentiment of the United
States. Let us assume that only a fourth
part of thes<- are In a position to act as ;
collecting and distributing agents for a
national Dewey testimonial, and that
each one of those 500 is willing to pledge
Itself to the collection from Its readers
nnd the transmission to the appointed
trustees of the sum of $500 for that fund.
Surely this estimate is moderate enough
and surely the amount of each Individual
subscription, which must be strictly fixed
and limited. Is moderate enough to pre- j
vent any newspaper from exploiting the
admiral for the purpose of advertising
Itself. And yet the aggregate of the sub

ADMIRAL WATSON WILL
SAIL NEXT TUESDAY
WASHINGTON, May 12.â Admiral
Watson will sail from San Francisco
next Tuesday as a passenger on one
of the regular Pacific Mail steamships,
but cannot possibly reach Manila be
fore the middle of June. Orders have
been issued placing Captain A. S. Bar
ker in command of the Asiatic sauad
ron after Admiral Dewey's departure
until relieved by Rear Admiral Wat
son.
Captain Barker is now ln command
of the battleship Oregon, the pride of
the navy, and next to Admiral Dewey
is the senior officer on the station. He
is an officer of ability and discretion
and may be depended on to take good j
care of the interests of the United
States. He was assistant chief of the
Bureau of Navigation during Admiral
Ramsey's term of office and a member j
of the naval war or strategy board
during the Spanish war. At the close j
of the war he relieved Captain Clark j
of command of the battleship Oregon
and took that magnificent warship j
from New York around the Horn j
to Callao, Peru, and thence by
way of Honolulu to Manila, where she
arrived several weeks ago. As soon as
relieved of the command of the Asiatic
squadron by Rear Admiral Watson,
about the middle of June, Captain Bar
ker will start for home under a leave
of absence granted a few days ago.

THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, SATURDAY, MAY 13, 1899.
WILL CARE FOR
THEIR OWN DEAD
President's Suggestion
Declined.
â â¢ â
CHARLESTON, S. C, May 12.â With
a spirited and at times stormy session
the ninth annual reunion of the United
Confederate Veterans' Association was
to-day brought to a close. General
John B. Gordon, commander-in-chief,
and all the old officers were re-elected.
Louisville, Ky., was chosen as the next
place of meeting and the question of
Federal care of Confederate graves was
disposed of by the adoption of a sub
stitute resolution which declines the
President's suggestion, except as to
those graves located in the North, and
reserving to the women of the South
the duty of caring for those in the se
ceding States and Maryland. The
adoption of this resolution and the re
port that accompanied it precipitated
a debate which verged upon the sensa
tional, and at times much confusion
and disorder prevailed.
General Stephen D. Lee presented
the report of the committee on history,
which was unanimously adopted. This
was one of the most important matters
before the convention and the reading
of the report was listened to with close
attention. The report alluded to the
war with Spain as a factor in eradicat
ing the shadows of the war between
the States. It refers to the prompt
response of the Southern States to the
call for troops as showing to the whole
country the depth and fervor of South
ern patriotism. Continuing the report
says :
President McKinley displayed the spirit
of the American soldier when he chose
from bis former foes the gallant Wheeler,
the steadfast Fitzhugh Lee. the chivalrous
Butler and many others of our own
brave comrades, to marshal the hosts of
the Union. We are glad General Wheeler
had another opportunity to exhibit the
righting qualities of the Confederate sol
diers and that Lieutenant Hobson, Lieu
tenant Blue and Ensign Worth Bagley
In the navy showed the country of what
stuff our young men are made. Once more
the blood of the North and the South has
been poured out togetherâ no longer be
neath contending standards in the bitter
ness of war between the States, but now
beneath one Hag, to the glory of the
country. These dead, at least, belong to
us all. The last hateful memory that
could divide our country is buried with
them. About their graves kneels a new
nation, loving all her children everywhere
the same.
Alluding to the question of Confed
erate graves the report says:
The recent generous words of President
McKinley commending the Confederate
dead to the nation's care, are the expres
sions of a sentiment growing everywhere
that the deeds of the Confederate soldier
are the glory of the whole country, and
that his memory is worthy to be cherished
wherever self-sacrifice commands sym
pathy or brave actions strike a respon
sive chord in noble hearts.
The committee also alludes to the
race question and expresses the opinion
that the "race problem is not apt in
the future to be so blinding to a true
apprehension of Southern history," and
says:
"The recent movement to put the
supremacy of the more educated and
capable race upon a constitutional and
legal basis, thus banishing the specter
of misrule from our borders, is steadily
gaining progress." Continuing the re
port says:
The reception given our benevolent In
tentions in the Philippines is certainly of
a character likely to inspire a wholesome
respect. We are not likely in the future
to hear so much about the right of men
who have not yet learned to govern them
selves to govern others by their votes.
The doctrine of the Declaration of Inde
pendence, that governments derive their
just powers from the consent of the gov
erned, had something of a shock in the
war between the States, and the island
subjects of the United States will find lit
tle comfort in reading that celebrated in
strument by the light of subsequent his
tory. The difficulties of the race prob
lem abroad ought to bring a charitable
judgment of the same thing here at home,
and we may reasonably expect our North
ern brethren to meet us hereafter rather
in a spirit of inquiry than of rebuke. â â :- Â»-;
The convention adjourned sine die at

reception to the veterans was held and
an address was delivered by Colonel

ing the First Illinois Regiment. Colonel
Turner has been the guest of the city
during the reunion. A brilliant ball
was given to the sponsors, maids of
honor and daughters of the' Confe
deracy. The veterans and visitors . are
rapidly departing.
JUST SAVE!' HIMSELF.
David Jacks Owes His Life to His
Presence of Mind.
MONTEREY, May 12.â The Monterey
capitalist, David Jacks, narrowly escaped
death at Salinas while trying to board
the detached caboose of a freight train
to-day. He found the steps of the caboose
too high for him to reach from the ground
and began climbing over, the bumper on
the front end of the caboose. Just then
the train backed some disconnected cars
down to the caboose, and he would
have been crushed to atoms only he real
ized his dangerous position and threw
himself backward off the track. He
fell on the ground just as the train came
together. He suffered no. injuries beyond
a few bruises, and Is now quite recovered
from the shock. r. - v

San Clemente Rancher's

â . Â»
Special Dispatch to The Call.
SAN RAFAEL, May 12.â Mrs. Joseph
Silvera, the wife of a prosperous dairy
man living near San Clemente, was at
tacked last evening by an unknown
man, for whom the authorities are now
diligently searching. The unfortunate
woman was seriously injured and will
not recover from the effects of her
rough treatment for many days.
Early in the evening a stranger
walked into the house and asked for
something to eat. He was well dressed
and at first his appearance aroused no
apprehension. As soon as he had fin
ished eating he arose and demanded
that Mrs. Silvera produce all the money
and jewels on the premises. A refusal
infuriated the ruffian, who sprang upon
Mrs. Silvera and choked her. She was
then dragged out the front door and
hurled from the porch to the ground.
Despite her bruises, the plucky woman
ran back into the house after her as
sailant, who was opening the drawers
of a bureau. With an oath he struck
her on the face, felling her to the floor.
She was again dragged into the yard
and left lying unconscious.
When Silvera returned home thirty
minutes later he found his wife hardly
able to move on account of her bruised
condition, while her cheek was discol
ored from the blow. A physician was
summoned and the authorities notified,
but so far no trace of the malefactor
has been discovered. It is believed that
he is an ex-convict who has been plant
ing opium along the line of the new
Tiburon boulevard, as his description
tallies with that of the man seen skulk
ing in the brush near San Clemente the
j day Captain Birlem's men detected two
i convicts attempting to smuggle the
I drug into San Quentin Prison. The mi
i dignation aroused by the assault and
attempted robbery is so strong that if
the criminal is captured he will be in
i danger of summary punishment.
EAGER TO LEAVE
KOTZEBUE SOUND
Sad Experience of Gold
Seekers.
â Â« â
SKAGUAY, May 5 (via Seattle, May
12.)â Fysh and W. Williams
reached Dawson on April 15 on foot
from Kotzebue Sound. Fysh and Wil
liams formed two of a party of eight
een who left lowa last summer and
joined the big rush which landed 2000
people on the various rivers of Kotze
bue Sound. The party established win
ter .quarters 300 miles up the Kowak
River. After untold hardships and la
bor, they decided that there was no
gold in the country. On February 19
Fysh and Williams struck out over the
divide to Koyukuk, thence to Dawson,
a distance of 1500 miles.
Mr. Fysh reports that Kotzebue
Sound is thickly peopled with an intelli
gent race of Indians closely resembling
the Japanese in habits and physique.
The principal diet heretofore has been
dried salmon, but the abundance of
white man's food last summer was the
cause ef laying away less than half a
crop of salmon. The result this winter
was that the illy nourished natives
have been dying' off like sheep. He
says the opening of the Bering Sea
will see the exodus of the last white
man and only a scattering of cabins
will mark the scene of one of the most
disastrous gold hunting expeditions
since Cortes landed on the coast of
Florida.
"â . W. I. Siegel, a United States mall car
rier, arrived from Dawson to-day with
165 pounds of mail matter from points
on the American side of the Yukon,
commencing at St.. Michael.
Captain Wallace Langley returned
from Victoria to-day with a grant for
the construction of the Atlin Short
Line Railway* and : Navigation Com
pany, a narrow gauge road of two and
a quarter miles to connect steamboat
navigation between Lake Bennett and
Atlin. c ' , '. -
Thomas C. Bennett, purser of the
steamer Nora, who killed James Cowie
at White Horse Rapids last August,
has been acquitted of the charge of
murder. .'.'Vr^V, -'^ b^-r '~7l : '':\
John Nelson of San Diego. Cal., died
at Dawson from scurvy on. April 13.
E. D. Stratford, special agent of the
United States Land Office, is here to
make a selection of sites for. a. public
building. From here he goes to Sitka.
The Skaguay Chamber of Commerce
last night sent official invitations to
President McKinley, the Secretary of
the Interior and the American members
of the High Joint Commission to visit
Skaguay this summer to study the
boundary question x and- the j need of
some sort of self-government.
Norman Rant, ex-Gold Commissioner
of the Atlin district, who arrived from
Victoria to-day. brings an order from
Minister of Mines Humes to the effect
that the opening of the mining season
is to be extended from June 1 to July 1.
He also brings word that Humes would
come up before the end of the month
with a commission of arbitration, be-
fore which all controversies â  about
ownership of claims in Atlin district
are to be settled. :
VOTING MACHINE TO
BE TESTED AT STOCKTON
STOCKTON, May 12.â The Ellis voting
machine Is to have a practical ; test at the
municipal election to be held here next
Tuesday. With the consent of the offi
cers one of the machines will be set up in
the polling place' of the First Precinct of
the First Ward. As soon as the voter has
deposited - his ballot he will be asked to
step to the machine i and vote his ticket
again upon ; it. A man. competent to ex
plain its workings will be on hand and an
effort will be made to get in a full vote
In order to ascertain how It will tally
with the results of the regular count In
that precinct. The test will not be offi
cial in any way, but it will be a fair trial
of the qualities of the machine.

Special Dispatch to The Call.
SANTA BARBARA, May 12.â The
Southern Pacific coast" line right of way
through the great'Holllst^- estate, that
is one obstacle responsible for the em
bargo that C. P. Huntington threatens
to put on railroad building, hinges on
the willingness of the railroad people
to pay $4400 to construct an archway
over a roadway on â  the Santa Anita
ranch. J. T. Richards, attorney and
trustee of the estate, in a written state
ment says the Southern Pacific has
been given twenty-five miles of right
of way over the ranch on condition that
it will build this archway to enable pas
sage from one side of the road to the
other. .There will be a deep fill, prob
ably forty feet, near the ranch house,
and it is under this that the arch is
wanted. The railroad people have re
fused flatly to build the arch, but fall
back on the Santa Barbara County
right of way committee to settle the
dispute and give them free passage
through the county, the Hollister es
tate included. Members of the Hollis
ter family declare they will not yield
to the demands of Huntington, as the
damage to the property will be far
greater than the cost of building the
arch, and twenty-five miles of right of
way over the lands is estimated to be
worth $10,000, which they have agreed
i to give.
In answer to the Morning Press tele
gram to C. P. Huntington, asking
whether or not the report was true that
work had been stopped, the following
I message was received late this even
ing:
Morning Press, Santa Barbara: No
truth in the report.
C. . P. HUNTINGTON.
The Southern Pacific Company, al
though threatening to discontinue the
work of construction, has had almost a
full force at work all day. The com
pany's officials here have circulated re
ports that it is due to the actions of
Louis G. Dreyfus and the Hollister es
tate that Huntington is about to sus
pend work. They to-day . made public
the Dreyfus letter to their contractors
and give it out that unless Dreyfus
came to their terms the road would
stop at his property three miles north
west of Ell wood. Dreyfus' letter reads:
You are hereby cautioned against tres
passing on my property, the Eagle Can
yon rancho, and you are notified not to
enter thereon. The Southern Pacific
Company nor any other company ha's no
right of way across the said property,
where the surveyors of said company
have surveyed a route for a railroad bed.
You are also notified not to cross any of
the said lands, unless proper authority be
obtained from me, nor to attach your
stock to any fencing on the premises, nor
to camp on any part of the property, or
on the county road crossing - the said
property, which road Is my property, ex
cepting for the purposes of a road to be
used by the general public. If you dis
regard this notice you are notified that
you will be held responsible for all dam
ages.
This letter was forwarded to railroad
headquarters on May 8, and immediate
ly letters came from Huntington to th<Â»
right of way committee here. Dreyfus'
action has been the sole topic of con
versation on the streets to-day and sev
eral attempts to hold an indignation
meeting have been made, although not
successful. This evening Dreyfus,
when seen by The Call correspondent,
again denied that he had in any way
hindered the company. He said its at
torney called on him several weeks ago
in regard to the right of way through
his property and he gave his terms,
which have, all been ignored by the
company, He has received no com
munication from the railroad â¢ officials
up to this time. * . \
Public opinion is against Dreyfus, but
at the same time it . considers that
Huntington is attempting a big bluff on
the people.
GARDNER AND FLAHERTY
r^IGHT TO A DRAW
Though Badly Handicapped, the
"Omaha Kid" Makes the Bet
ter Showing.
NEW YORK, May 12.â Oscar Gardner,
the "Omaha Kid," and Martin Flaherty
of Lowell, Mass., fought a 25-round draw
at the New Broadway Athletic Club to
night in the presence of a crowd that
packed the auditorium and held overflow
meetings in the aisles and entries. It was
an unsatisfactory fight from the stand
point of the spectators, for there was a
sameness about the tactics of the men
throughout and flashes of brilliancy were
infrequent. Gardner was the cleverer,
man of the two and outboxed his oppo
nent at every stage of the game. He was
handicapped, however, in that he had an
unhealed cut over his left eye and came
into the ring with a big bandage around
his head. In action the bandage slipped
down and until he took it off he was at
times playing bllndman's buff. Later In
the contest the cut was reopened and bled
so freely that his left eye was at -times
blinded. He also seemed to show the ef
fect of too much fighting of late, for he
lacked the dash and vim that have
brought him so many victories.
Flaherty had the advantage of height,
weight and reach and made the pace in
most of the rounds, but his work was in
effective. He constantly over-reached and
looked wild alongside of the little man,
who was generally inside his punches or
under them. He displayed a very dan
gerous left hand, but lacked Judgment of
distance to make It useful. -â¢ â¢ j

A Communication to Be Sent to

Interesting Notes.
Major General Merriam, U. S. A., Is
unpopular with the labor union? through- j
out the country .sine his edict against
the employment of union miners in the
famous Coeur I'Alene mining district. On
Thursday evening the Building Trades
'Council decided to make an Investigation
'into the troubles at Wardner, Idaho, with
a view to fixing the blame for the recent
troubles, and It also condemned the gen
eral for his action ln accusing union min
ers of being guilty of all the lawless
ness.
The Labor Council at Its weekly session
last evening followed In the footsteps of
Its afflicted organization. I: was unani
mously agreed that a committee draw tip
resolutions "condemning the. expressions
of General Merrlam, classifying labor or
ganizations as criminal conspiracies, and
protesting against the order Issued by
him prohibiting the employment of union
men in th Coeur d'Alene mines."
The motion was introduced by A. Fu
ruseth of the Sailors' Union; and In mak
ing it he delivered a warm speech, crit
icizing General Merrlam and the admin
istration. I*. H. McCarthy and a num
ber of other members followed In fiery
speeches along the same line. The reso
lutions will be adopted at the next meet
ing and will be published broadcast
throughout the United States In labor
journals and other periodicals, in the
nope that it may arouse the union labor
ers to an appreciation of the "unfair
irr-atment accorded them by General Mer
riam."'
Secretary Rosenberg" was Instructed to
communicate with the Treasury Depart
ment and ask that the request or the
Mechanics' Institute to land Filipinos in
this city for exhibition purposes be de
nied.
A delegate from the Sailors' Union re
ported that business was improving along
the water front; that nearly four-fifths
of the vessels leaving port are employing
sailors at union wages, and that the police
do not understand the law recently passed
which permits a sailor to leave a vessel
at his own pleasure. Attention was also
called to Serjeant Bonner's advice to of
ficers of various vessels anchored in the
stream to tire on any boat that came
alongside their ship for the purpose of
taking men off. The delegate further said
there was every indication that all ship
owners would bo compelled to pay union
rates this summer.
The Sailors' Union requested that the
Labor Council have its secretary commu
nicate with the Miners' Union at Nanai
mo, B. C., and ascertain what arrange
ments can be made to have coal carried
exclusively by ships employing union men.
This will be attended to at once.
A large amount of business was trans
acted by the council before adjournment.
Carpenters' Union No. reinitiated eight
new members last night and also d.s
cussed General Merrlam and the admin
istration. Heated speeches of condemna
tion were delivered and resolutions were
adopted. ____________________
ANOTHER FRAUD
ON THE REVENUE
COMES TO LIGHT
Internal Revenue Agents Thomas and
Towne have unearthed another gigantic
fraud against the Government on the
part of unscrupulous cigar manufacturers
in New York. New Jersey and Pennsylva
nia. The fraud does not consist in the use
of counterfeit revenue cigar' stamps, and
hence must have been going on for a long
time, the stamp* being genuine and there
being nothing about the boxes to attract
suspicion.
About a week ago Mr. Thomas discov
ered that certain manufacturers were dis
tributing throughout the United States
sample boxes containing twenty-sir and
fifty-one cigars each, the stamps on the
boxes being for twenty-five and fifty each,
thus defrauding the Government out of
the tax on one cigar in each box, or out
of forty in each thousand cigars. The
Government woul<J not suffer much if the
number of these boxes was not large, but
when it runs into the tens of thousands
distributed over every Stale in the Union
it is a serious matter.
It is apparent that no manufacturer
would care to run the risk of detection in
a fraud of this kindâ the risk involving
the confiscation of his factory and of his
entire stock â merely to save a few dollars.
The saving must have run up into the
thousands.
.The agents began their seizures a day
or two ago and succeeded ln getting 6000
cigars ln Los Angeles. They are on the
track of othoTs in this city and expect
to round up many more thousands to-day.
Mr. Thomas was the- first person to
make this important discovery and he im
mediately wired to the head of the reve
nue department in Washington and there
are Indications that within the next few
days seizures will be made in every State
In the Union. J

OWm?Â«"rÂ»O^)3^'o^?Â»tÂ«<^tÃ
The way is clear for the eradication of
the greatest , evil of Chinatownâ the
breeder of all other evils in that quarter.
In a letter mailed yesterday to M. M. i
Foote, who is secretary of the Vigilant I
Society, Mayor Phelan admits that "it is
made the duty of certain city officers, in- I
eluding the Mayor, to give notice to all I
violators of the act approved March 31,
1566, for the suppression of Chinese houses
of 111-fame." He adds that he will be !
pleased to comply with Mr. Foote's re- '
quest and give the required notice on re
ceipt of the Information Mr. Foote is un
derstood to possess concerning the loca
tion, the ownership and the inmates of
such houses.
The information the Mayor desires will
be furnished,, although Mr. Foote says it
could just as easily be obtained by the
city's executive and the Chief of Police,
who Is also called upon to move under
the act, by applying to any member of
the Chinatown squad. In the same spirit,
however, he says, as that in which he
wrote the letter to the Mayor pointing out
an evil' that that official should know ex
isted and indicating the law that provided
for its eradication, he will gladly furnish
Mr. Phelan with a List of the houses of
prostitution in Chinatown, the names of
their inmates and the owners. It will
remain then, he says, for the Mayor and
the Chief of Police to move under the law,
or to be moved by writ of mandamus un
der the law. The. Mayor says in his reply
to Mr. Foote'that he will act as required.
Chief Lees, who has not been officially
informed of any complaint against the
dens in Chinatown, which he does not
deny exist, refuses to say what he will do.
"You know what I'll do when the time
comes," he said last night to a reporter
for The CalL v, . â¢ . . :;
No matter what the Chief does, the in
formation that the Mayor has signified his
willingness to accept will be forthcoming
ln a few days, as soon as the agents of
the Vigilant . Society make their reports
to the directory,' and In as short a, time as
possible it will -be turned over to thÂ«i
Mayor, who will undoubtedly be as good
as his - word' and serve on the proprietors
and inmates of the one hundred and fifty
odd dens warnings to vacate, at the same
time notifying the property owners that
unless ' their' -premises are purged of the
evil they will be liable to criminal prose
cution. Then will come the turn of the
Chief of Police. If the slaveholders ignore
the notices and the owners of the -prop
erty in which the dens are located refuse
to obey the law It will be the duty of
Chief Lees tee awake to a law that gov
erns him and to do his duty long neg
lected. He must either Issue orders for
the arrest of the proprietors of the houses
and the inmates and must Bee that they
are arrested or he will be dragged into
court himself.
That is the status to-day of the recently
Inaugurated crusade against the dens of
vice where highbinders breed and where
murder Is plotted. At the headquarters
of the Vigilant Society In the Sherman
block there is rejoicing over the fact that
a law, no matter how innocuous from dis
use, needs only to be dusted to be ef
fective.
THEBMALITO HOME.
Odd Fellows Decide Not to Remove
It Work of Bebekahs.
The consideration of the supplementary
report of the special committee on the
condition of the home at Thermalito and
the advisability of continuing it there
occupied the attention of the representa
tives all day yesterday, and when It was
time to adjourn at 5 o'clock the debate
was not closed, so an adjournment was
taken until 8 o'clock In the evening. At
that hour the discussion was resumed,
and at 10 o'clock a resolution was Intro
duced authorizing the trustees of the
home to remove it and secure a new site.
This was lost by a vote of 298 to 192. An
adjournment was then taken until 9
o'clock this morning.
At the session of the California Assem
bly held yesterday there were received a
number of additional reports from dele
gates as to the condition of their respec
tive lodges, and these showed prosperity.
The resolutions presented the day before
as to. endeavors to secure further power
from the Sovereign Grand Lodge to en
able the Rebekah division of Odd Fellow
ship "to obtain recognition on the floor of
that body and to be able to legislate as to
benefits were adopted.
It was decided to reduce the age of ad
mission to the Orphans' Home at Gilroy
from 16 to 14 years. It was also decided
to- give the authorities of the home au
thority to return to guardians any chil
dren who might be ungovernable. There
are none such there now, but this action
was taken to guard against possibilities.
A resolution was offered having for its
purpose a. reduction of the basis of repre
sentation to the assembly. Action will be
taken on that to-day.
A motion to allow mileage to delegates
was lost. , v :
The Cooper Inquest.
Coroner Hill began an inquest yester
day morning Into the cause of the death
of Joseph Cooper, the Powell-street tailor
who was asphyxiated in his bed last Mon
day night. Mrs. Phcbe Abrams, Bessie
Cooper and Joseph Cooper, children of
the deceased, testified as to the finding
of their father's body.
The stopcock of the gasjet was partly
turned on and it was loose, thus giving
strong color to the. theory that the death
was accidental. Dr. Josef I. Monson tes
tified that he had been attending the de
ceased for several months for heart dis
ease, and believed that he had died from
that ailment.
Morgue Surgeon Zabala said that the
autopsy revealed the fact that death was
due to asphyxiation. The Inquest will be
resumed this morning.
THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, SATURDAY, MAY 13, 1899.
TRIED TO END
HIS EXISTENCE
WITH A RAZOR
Major J. J. Barnett in
Trouble.
â â¦
NO MONEY AND NO PROSPECTS
â Â» â
ONE TIME PROMINENT IN THE
BUCKLEY BANKS.
*
Eight Years Ago He Was One of the
Principal Actors in a Tragedy
That Has Weakened His
Mind.
â¢
Major J. J. Barnett, an old-time politi
cian, attempted yesterday morning to
commit suicide at 1117 Broadway, where
he resides with some friends, by slashing
his left wrist six times with a razor. He
was taken to the Receiving Hospital in
the patrol wagon and his wounds were
attended to. Barnett said that if his
friend had not discovered him while he
was slashing his wrist he would have
killed himself. He was tired of life, as he
was without money and had no prospect
of earning any.
"I am a lifelong friend of Chris BncTt
ley," he Bald, "and previous to the last
election I went to Buckley and asked him
to use his influence to get me a position.
He said he would, but I was not success
ful. I called upon him a few days ago
and again asked him to look out for me
after the next election, but he did not
give me any hope of success, and I con
cluded the best thing for me was to die."
As. Barnett was very weak from the
loss of blood ho was prevailed upon to
remain in the hospital until something
could lie done by his friends for him.
Barnett was one of the principal actors
in a tragedy about eight years ago. He
and May Castree, a handsome woman of
the town, were infatuated with each
other and one night after they had been
to supper and had gone to the woman's
room over a saloon on Mason street, the
woman saton his knee and suggested that
they should both die. They talked the
â  matter over and finally Barnett consent
ed. He had a revolver In his pocket and
after they had taken an affectionate fare
well of each ether Barnett placed the
muzzle of the revolver over the woman's
heart and. fired, killing her instantly. He
then placed the weapon over his own
' heart and fired, but the bullet missed the
i fatal spot by the fraction of an inch. He
' did not recover for several months, and
when he did lie was arrested on the
I charge of murder. After a trial before a
| jury in the Superior Court he was ac
; quitted. Since then he has not been In
| exactly his right mind, it is thought from
I brooding over the tragedy.

MB. HUNTINGTON'S BANQUET.
Will Entertain His Fourteen Heads
of Departments To-Night.
C. P. Huntington will be host to-night
at a banquet to be tendered the fourteen
heads of departments by whom he was
entertained at the Palace two weeks ago,
and their assistants. The affair is to take
place at Mr. Huntington's California
street residence and promises to be one of
the swellesl affairs of the season.
To-night's affair will probably be Mr.
Huntington's last appearance at an event
of this sort during his present stay in the
city. His annual trip has already been
drawn out to a greater length than he had
anticipated, as has been the visit of Mr.
Speyer, the New York financier, who came
out to attend the annual election of the
Central Pacific directorate. Both of them
will hurry their departure. Mr. Speyer
would have started yesterday but for the
banquet to-night, where he will be one of
the guests of honor. As It Is he will leave
on Sunday and it Is likely that Mr. Hunt
ington will depart at the same time, as a
joint inspection of the Western properties
of the Central and Southern Pacific sys
tems was part of their original itinerary.
Gingerbread Fete Beceipts.
At the last meeting of the board of
managers of the Children's Hospital the
sum of $2800 was reported netted by the
recent gingerbread fete. Out of this $1100
must be expended for fire escapes.

A BAY COLLISION.
, - â¢
The Schooner Volant and the Pilot
Boat Gracie S Bump Each Other
With Bad Results.
The schooner Volant, in command of
Captain Krog, sailed in last night from
Grays Harbor, and when opposite Meiggs
wharf collided with the pilot-boat Gracie
S. which was lying at anchor. The Gracie
B had her Jibboom, headgear, etc., carried
away and sustained a great deal of dam
age on deck.
The Volant, after getting clear of the
pilot-boat, drifted on to Meiggs wharf,
carrying away the boat davits, smashing
several boats and doing considerable
other damage. Tin- Volant had her head
gear, main rigging and mainsail carried
away and also sustained a lot of damage
on deck.
Lieutenant Nash Banqueted.
Lieutenant Nash, who has charge of
the rifle team of the Police Department,
was yesterday banqueted by his sharp
shooters at Shell Mound Park: About
twenty policemen were present* Speeches
were made by Lieutenant Nash. Police
men Phillips and "Mauser" Rankin. The
latter made a decided hit in his latest
creation, "How Mulcahey Lost His Job."
Previous to the banquet the team held its
regular shoot. High scores were made
by the men, showing that they had not
lost their cleverness with the new rifle.

Nm^-so market st. aF.SSrGAS COMPANY
HAS RECOURSE
TO THE COURTS
Brings Suit to Compel Payment
of a Bill at $1.75 Rate.
Agrees to Refund Any Difference Between
Amount Charged* and That Finally
Fixed by Court.
â¢
The San Francisco Gas and Electric '
Company has been at last forced into the j
open. Failing to secure from the Board :
of Supervisors a rate for their gas in j
keeping with what they claim is a reason- !
able charge for lighting, the officers of the |
company have had recourse to the courts.
Late yesterday afternoon a complaint
was filed in the office of County Clerk j
Deane against Major W. B. Hooper, man- j
ager of the Occidental Hotel, alleging that j
he is indebted to the gas company for i
Jejol for gas supplied during April at the j
rate of $1 75 per 1030 f-?et.
The attorneys are Bishop & Wheeler, j
with whom is associated E. S. Pillshury, j
for the gas company. Mr. Bishop filed the |
papers himself, selecting apparently a j
time when the reporters had left their
posts of duty for the day. The complaint !
is a purely formal document, giving no j
intimation as to what issues will be made I
during the trial. A creditor owing over j
$.>Â») was selected to the end that the case j
would go at once to the Superior Court,
thus saving all unnecessary delay.
In the meantime the company is collect- j
ing its April bills at the rate of $1 75 per j
1000 feet, although some consumers are j
refusing to pay until the legal rate has
been determined. To placate these the
company is s-tamping upon their bills the
following agreement:
If any less rate than that herein
charged is hereafter adjudged to be
legal this company agrees to refund
the difference.
â¢
Attorney Pillshury of counsel for the
â¢company; affirms that the case will be ex
pedited as much as possible on the part >
of his clients, as they wish to know what ;
is the legal rate they may chars-. It will
be prosecuted vigorously by the brightest
men at the bar, the company making sure
that its case will not suffer in their hands. :
"The filing of the suit against Major
Hooper; manager of the Occidental Hotel, ;
means that the gas company has gone to
the courts to have settled legally and defi
nltely the rate it may charge consumers."
said Attorney E. S. Pillsbury last night, ;
"We will expedite the case as much as lies
in our power, as it is to the interest of the ,
gas company to know the exact status of
the case. Major Hooper was made ilefend
ant because his bill was sufficiently large
to take the case to the Superior Court, i

Thirty-nine candidates will receive con
firmation at the Bush street Synagogue
next Sunday evening. A particularly
beautiful service has 'been arranged, and
the ceremonies will be very impressive,
from the fact that each of the applicants
will have some specific function to per
form different from the other? in the large
class. The interior of the edifice will oe
handsomely decorated and the music for
the oecasio'n has been composed especially
by Miss Regina Jacobson, recently re
turned from Australia.
This will be the largest number of appli
cants ever confirmed at one time in any
Jewish synagogue on the Pacific Coast.
Originally there were forty-one in the
class, but owing to sickness, two candi
dates. Miss Leah Kaitin and William Wil
liams, will be unable to receive the bless
ings of the sacrament. The reeuiar les
tival service will compose the first part of
the ceremony and will commence at 7
o'clock sharp. It will be conducted by
Cantor D. Meyerson.
Confirmation will begin at half-past 7.
Nt which Rev. Isidore Myers, the rabbi of
the congregation, will officiate. The ran
s.oal prcgiamme will be rendered under
the direction of Professor Dellapiane.
Next Sunday is termed, according to the
Jewish law. the "Feast of Weeks." It is
in commemoration of the anniversary of
the giving of the law on Mount Sinia. and
is known in Hebrew rituals as Shovuos
Sunday.. The day has beÂ« n chosen on ac
count of Its significance for thÂ° adminis

OAKLAND. .May 12.â The new nealth
board held its first regular meeting to
night. A large crowd of politicians was
present expecting to see the patronage
distributed. A motion by- Dr. Maher that
the matter of patronage be postponed in
definitely was carried. The terms of the

We might have sued on a smaller bill, but
if it were heard in the Justice Court it
would of necessity have to go to a higher
court before being finally accepted.
"The consumers will lose i thing if it
is finally decided that the il 1') rate was
legally fixed. On each bill the company
wii! stamp a promise to refund any dif
ference between the rate paid by a house
holder and that which the court will say
is the rate to which the company is en
titled.
"As to what issues will be brought up
during the trial it is impossible to fore
cast. The main fight will hinge on the
question whether the Supervisors have
authority to fix rates, and if. having the
power to fix them, they have also vested
in them the power to rescind their action.
On April 3 the boar-VfixtHl a rate of $1 10,
and a week later rescinded that action.
This left the rate at the former figures.
$1 75 per thousand feet."
Mr. Pillshury was asked if this was a
"friendly" suit. He laughed and said:
"If you think there is any friendship in
this just go down and interview Major
Hooper. I'll venture to say he is the
angriest man in town. I don't blame him
for refusing to pay the bill. He is man
ager of the hotel and as such is responsi
ble for its affairs being handled in a busi
nesslike manner. He was selected rom a
number of people whose bills for April re
main unpaid."
George K. Hooper, who represents Ma
jor W. B. Hooper, manager of the Occi
dental, was surprised when told of the
filing of the suit. "This is news to me,"
he said. "I know the gas hill was due
and that it had not been paid. Major
Hexiper did not feel justified in paying so
large a bill when there was a e-pieetion as
to the legality of the rate of $1 75 per
thousand feet for which the bill was ren
dered. We have been approached by per
sons who wanted us to join the organiza
tion of people who ha pooled their issues
and were prepared to resist the -collection
of any gas bills rendered for more than
$1 10 per thousand. The persons who
called on us had no credentials and could
not show any list of firms whom they
represented. I do not know why we
should tee singled out above any one else
as the ones en whom the validity of the
recent action of the Supervisors should
be tested. .
"In Justice to ourselves we did not
think It right to pay a bill in which there
was so marked a difference between the
charge made and that .which seemed to
be the legal charge. Like others, we with
held payment until such time as the ques
tion of rates was decided. We surely did
not expect to be defendants in an action
of this character. No Dapers have been
served on us and we do not know how
matters stand."
