MRS. CRAVEN CONFIDENT OF
ULTIMATE VICTORY.
Judge Denson Makes a Long Argu
ment to Show the Court His
Client's Right to Inter
vene.
~ "-
In the courtroom that has resounded
for months with the voices of the numer
ous counsel in the now celebrated Fair
will case the attorneys gathered again
yesterday, somewhat lessened in numbers
but still fresh, to continue the argument
on Mrs. Nettie K. Craven's application to
be permitted to Intervene for the purpose
of contesting the will of the late James G. j
Fair, dated September 21. 1894, which is
known as the "trust will."
Mrs. Craven was in court all day yes
terday, and took decided interest in the
dry legal arguments of her attorneys, i
Her application is based upon precisely
the same grounds as that filed on behalf â¢
of Charles i.. Fair, the son. It was shown
during the discussion that under the stat- i
ute. after a will has been admitted to pro
bate without contest, any person may in- |
tervene in an action or proceeding: before j
the trial who has an interest in the con
troversy. Charles L. Fair's application j
to move to set aside the probate of the j
will was tiled on the _â â  of November, |
ISS7. which was the last day of the year I
provided by the statute. Since that time
the controversy has remained dormant.
A few weeks ago Mrs. Craven applied
to the court for permission to intervene in
that contest for the pur; of setting
aside the probate of be will of September
21. Judge Troutt. to whom the applica
tion was made, directed service to be |
made upon the attorneys for the heirs and ;
executors, ordering them to show cause, j
if any they had. why the petition should ,
not be filed. Mrs. Craven's application ,
was opposed by the attorneys of all the j
children and of the executors, nd ft was
on this point that the i arguments of j
Thursday and yesterday were made.
In the opening argument authorities j
were presented to how that where the
contest had been kept alive by the action
of Charles L. Fair any one interested in I
the estate would have a right to come in.
The attorneys for the executors and j
children, on the other hand, claim that
Mrs. Craven's right to intervene was
barred by the statute of limitations and :
that her petition was gaily defective
in that it did not state facts sufficient to j
constitute a legal cause of action.
Mr. Foote on Wednesday made a strong i

KIND WORDS FOB, FIELD.
University of California Expresses
Grief at His Death.
The committee appointed by the Aca
demic Senate of the University of Call- j
fornia to draft a suitable memorial no
tice of the death of Justice Field has sub- i
mitted a report. It is signed by George
Davidson, Charles W. Slack and G. H. j
Howison, and expresses deep sympathy
for the widow of the deceased. In part
it is as follows:
At such a time, we. as Callfornians, cannot
forget th.- debt our State owes to his public
labors. In plonker days when crowds of eager
gold-hunters Invaded a land governed by Span
ish codes and covered by Mexican land grants .
a condition of affairs arose altogether unpre- ,
cedented. Daily conflicts of Interests were too ,
often settled by violent means. At this June- i
ture his legislative work, guided by far-sighted <
sagacity, gave the force of law to the cus
toms and usages of the miners, and not only
brought order out of chaos, bat laid the foun- :
dation of the present mining laws of our coun
try. Ever mindful of the poor and struggling, ,
he orie_inate?d the law which prevented the j
creditor from seizing the tools and implements
by which the artisan earned his daily bread. â¢
He organized the judicial system of our State, j
drew up its first codes of civil and criminal pro
cedure, and fear many years administered jus
tice on its Supreme Bench.

A DISCOURSE ON REST.
Dr. Henry Gibbons Jr. Tells How to
Be Restful.
Dr. Henry Gibbons Jr. delivered the last
of the Lane lectures for the year at Cood
er Medical College last night. His suo
ject was "Rest and How to Take it," and
a large audience was agreeably enter
â¢ tamed by his discourse. The lecture was
enlivened by a number of inter-lantern
. slides, the negatives of which were taken
by the doctor himself in various parts of
the State where he has sojourned during
the last sixteen years. The views com
prised pictures of scenery in the Santa
I Cruz Mountains. Coronado Beach. Yosem
lte Valley and other places, all of which
he said were ideal spots to obtain rest in
its best form. Â Â
"All the animal and vegetable world
took rest in some stage of its growth,
said Dr. Gibbons, "and mankind could
well take a ideal from the inferior or
' rm - , , â¢_
\ll the animal and vegetable world
k rest in some stage of its growth,
i Dr. Gibbons, "and mankind could
1 take a lesson from the inferior or
ders. Rest is a word of great import. It
means a relief from toil.. labor and study.
and is a respite from care, release from
i pain and surcease of sorrow. Recreation
I is absolutely necessary for our well being
and is as essential to our existence as is
l eating. Rest favors and hastens repair
| when exercise has been excessive. It Is
! invaluable in the cure of all diseases and
I is in itself a potent cure. The advantages
jof rest are more apparent in surgical
ca=es One way cure of all diseases and
itself a potent cure. The advantages
Â«t are more apparent in surgical
One way of obtaining rest is W
going to the country. A radical change of
' scenery is necessary to blot out the old
thoughts and instill new ones into .our
minds. In California can be found com
bined all the beautiful scenery for which
certain European countries are famous.
NATIVE DAUGHTERS.
.-.Â«â¢_,: â -â  _____ _ ;
They Will Observe Their Memorial
Day on Monday Next.
Monday, the loth of May. is the memo
rial day set apart by the order of Native
Daughters of the Golden West on which
to hold appropriate exercises in memory
of those members of the order who have
passed away. On that day every parlor in
the State will manifest respect and sym
pathy for those who in the past were com
panions in the noble work In which the
entire membership is engaged. During
the day committees from each parlor will
visit the silent cities of the dead in which
are at rest those who have gone before
and strew over their graves sweet flowers
to demonstrate that, though passed away.
they are not forgotten.
The following named have been elected
delegates to the Grand Parlor that is to
hold its session in Woodland next month:
Occidental Parlor if Eurekaâ Mrs. M.

"JUST LIKE GAGE."
READERS of the interior press just now might
suppose that our Governor had been cut up
into tempting or tempted slices, for the
purpose of frying him over a hot fire is
going on all over the State. It is not
the comparatively modern and delicate system
of grilling that has been adopted, but the old
fashioned method of the pioneer days, and the siz
zling and the sputtering of the hot railroad grease are'
vocalizing and perfuming the air in every direction.
It seems that the Governor determined that the
Huntington leprosy and the smallpox ought not to
be allowed to spread at the same time. Being deeply
infected with the first disease, which had become too
prevalent for immediate extirpation, he addressed
himself to the second and roused up Dr. Matthews,
the president of the Board of Health, late at night so
that before morning he might arrange to check its
advancing march at Fort Yuma. For this extra
labor he received due credit in the expressive words,
"Just like Gage." There may have been some strategy
in this nocturnal visitation, for vaccination, which
successfully resists smallpox, communicates the Hun
tington leprosy in all its forms, including elephan
tiasis, or the swollen corruption of the body and of
the head.
The refrain, "Just like Gage,"' was quickly appro
priated and disseminated. One paper marked the
antithetical exercise of one of his executive functions
when he vetoed a bill allowing our bright women,
among them the Native Daughters of the Golden
West, to vote for School Trustees, and signed an act
legalizing prize-fights. Others criticized his approval
of the anti-cartoon and signature bills, the uncon
stitutionality of which as a lawyer and the absurdity
of which as a citizen he must have well known, as
indeed he admitted when in the face of such legisla
tion he treated the report that he was about to go
into the newspaper business as ridiculous. Still others
resented the "scoop" he gave the Examiner and its
Canadian annex of his intention to appoint Dan
Burns a Senator of the United States if Matt Quay
should be seated. Then, under the heading, among
others, of "Peanut Politics," he was not only fried
but burned for his ostentatious and vulgar rudeness
to Senator Perkins at the Los Angeles celebration of
the San Pedro victory over C. P. Huntington. His
conduct in this particular instance indicated that he
was not quite satisfied with the anti-railroad triumph,
which Senator Perkins was so largely instrumental in
securing. His loyalty to Los Angeles and his affec
tion for C. P. Huntington evidently conflict.
In each instance of this culinary operation the jus
tification of the chef de cuisine was "Just like Gage."
Other things may be also mentioned that are just like
him. His acted confessions of absolute absorption by
the railroad are rapidly accumulating. He began with
the Board of Health. He continued with the Board
of Equalization. Now he has filled the place of Sena
tor Boggs, deceased, on the State Board of Agricul
ture by the appointment of that distinguished farmer
and horticulturist, Grove L. Johnson, whose great
achievements have been the farming of the revenues,
the grafting of Huntington shoots upon the Tree of
State, the pruning of the constitutional rights of citi
zens and of the press, and the application of the rail
road bacilli to the roots of our civilization.
Henry T. Gage is surely a daisy in his particular
line, but the line itself produces a network of slimy
discolorations. He is one of the examples of indi
vidual political suicide. His notoriety has destroyed
his popularity. His influence in the disintegration of
his party and in turning the State over to Democratic
fusion has been prematurely exhausted. Charity,
however, would like to furnish him with a remedy
for his serious ailments. As the surgeons have suc
ceeded in taking reefs in the human stomach, per
haps they might succeed in contracting swelled heads
and replacing coarse brains by mental conductors of
a finer quality. This is apparently the only chance
for our gubernatorial phenomenon.
With Governor Gage sneaking with W. F. Herrin
by a secret door from the Palace Hotel, and Railroad
Commissioners Laumeister. and Blackstock clinking
glasses with the railroad attorney at his home, it be
gins to look as if the Southern Pacific Company, in
all truth, is out of politics and has seized the State
government.
______
The young school teacher of the Shoshone Indian
reservation who drew her revolver upon two men to
defend one of her pupils evidently believed that in
teaching the young idea how to shoot she might take
a few lessons herself.
Railroad Commissioner Laumeister said he would,
in shame, leave the State if he voted for the Black
stock resolution. If he really meant it a suggestion
of how to keep him out would be timely.

THE STREET RAILROAD PROBLEM.
THE CALL has placed fairly before the public
the separate proposals of the Market Street
Railway Company and their combined effect, if
sanctioned by the Board of Supervisors, in firmly in
trenching an obnoxious monopoly in the most
densely populated business and residence sections of
San Francisco. It has also recognized the wants and
the rights of the northeastern quarter, so long neg
lected, which were impressed into the service of the
corporation, so that, under cover of supplying a
proper and imperative, demand, it might impregnably
fortify its position before the new charter becomes
operative.
It is useless to recapitulate the details of the at
tempted grab. They are already well understood.
The vital question is, What does the Board of Super
visors intend to do? How can it meet an actual ne
cessity for the extension and improvement of street
railway facilities, with entire fairness to this aggres
sive monopoly, and yet without throwing away
municipal property and sacrificing the common in
terests its members are sworn to protect?
The Huntington argument, invoked for street rail
ways as well as for State lines and national high
roads, and almost flung at the people, is very short
and pointed â "We intend to help ourselves; how do
you propose to help yourselves?" This mode of set
tling public questions is irritating and almost unen
durable, but in the past it has been effective. It has
been quite humiliating as well as exasperating for
three hundred and fifty thousand inhabitants of a com
mercial metropolis to have to ask themselves how
much of their earnings were to be confiscated through
some of their representatives in the municipal legis
lature and to what extent municipal property was to
be donated to corporations in order that they might
use the resources of a community, gratuitously ac
quired, to extort enormous incomes from the indi
viduals of whom the community is composed.
If the present Board of Supervisors contains a ring
of seven, with absolute power to act, irrespective of
the minority and of the Mayor, and who are the mere
tools of the street railway monopoly, then there is no j
doubt that the seven can outvote the five. But, while
fully comprehending the pessimism of our citizens
predicated of past experience, The Call has not yet
become convinced that, in the year 1899, we" have one
of those rings. It prefer* to believe that the proposals
of the Market Street Railway Company are to be sub
jected to some honest and intelligent test.
On this assumption, then, what ought to be done?
The Call did not support the new charter, but when
that instrument received popular indorsement and leg- :
islative ratification it became the law, to be respected ;
and enforced as long as it constitutionally exists. I
The last expression of the public will, therefore, is
that no franchise or privilege should be granted ;
within the municipal limits except in conformity with
its provisions. If the Board of Supervisors accepts j
the instruction of the ballot-box and of the Legisla- j
ture, the entire subject ought to be postponed till
after January I, 1900.
But suppose it be admitted that the exigency of the :
situation, under the promptings of the North Central
Improvement Association and the Merchants' Asso- |
ciation, will not admit of eight months' delay, and j
that pome immediate action must be taken, what .
should it be? The answer is very plain. If the ob
ject to be attained is to connect the northeastern part
of San Francisco with the Potrero and the interven
ing territory, all that is essential or that could be
honestly required is that the application of the Mar
ket Street Railway Company for a new connecting
franchise on Taylor street, between Geary and Post
streets, be granted, and that, if the law permits, it
be awarded the privilege of using electricity on its
entire line from Brannan street .to Montgomery
street and along that street and through Washington
and Jackson streets to Sansome street, and perhaps
on Sansome street for one block. This would involve
'some material changes in the order for which the
company has petitioned in order to annex the privi
lege of using electricity to "franchises heretofore \
granted." It would eliminate Geary street from Tay
lor street to Grant avenue. That portion of the order
to which we have not specifically alluded, namely, on
Post street, from Taylor to Leavenworth street, along 1
Leavenworth street and across McAllister street to
City Hall avenue, and thence by way of Grove street,
with crossings on Larkin street and Market street, to
Bryant -street, if included in the electrical privilege,
would perfect the system and meet all the require
ments of the property-owners and public-spirited
citizens in the two associations to which we have re
ferred.
The demand for a new franchise from the intersec
tion of Geary street and Grant avenue to Bush street
and down Bush street to Sansome street, and the ap
plication for the privilege of using electricity on
Geary street, from Taylor street to Grant avenue, and
also on Sansome street, except .between. Washington
and Jackson streets, should be flatly and promptly de
nied. The application for this new franchise is the I
completion of the scheme to exclude competition, i
An electrical line on Sansome street, from Jackson ;
! to Market street, would interfere with heavy traffic ,
and would be an excuse or an opportunity for a sub- â 
I sequent attempt to secure electrical privileges on
Market street. The worst' "joker" in the plan is the
Geary street electrical proposition, and it will be ,
readily understood when the first watering of stock
and issue of bonds by the Market street railway com- I
bination is recalled. The company now has a cable !
, line, mainly' on Geary ' street, from Market street to I
the Golden Gate Park, the franchise for which will
expire in 1902. It also holds unused franchises on !
Geary street, from Grant avenue to Taylor street, i
which ought to have been forfeited long ago, under
section 2of the act of March 13, 1897. The new char- i
ter limits street railway franchises to twenty-five 'â¢
years, and compels the payment during the first five
years, now exempted from such taxation, of 3 per
cent, during the next ten years of 4 per cent, and dur
ing the .last ten years of five per cent, of the gross j
receipts. It also provides that at the end of the term
the road track and bed of the street railway, and ail 'its i
stationary fixtures, shall become the property of the i
municipality. It further secures to the Board of Su- i
j pervisors' the power "to regulate the rates of fare," !
and to its Finance Committee unrestricted opportu- 1
nities to expert the railway books. It also contains
numerous other binding securities for the protection
of the city and county, to which we cannot here ad
vert.
A renewal of the present Geary street franchise,
under the new charter, would bring the Market
Street Railway Company down to the actual value of
the property and would afford no opportunity for a !
repetition of its former strategy by watering stock â 
or by floating bonds. But section 2 of the act. of
March 13. 1897, at any time more than one year be
fore the expiration of the street railway franchise, per- j
mits its surrender, if accepted by the Board of Super- !
visors, and then it can be renewed. If the electrical \
privilege now asked on Geary street as an addition to !
the unused franchises be awarded, then that posses- i
sion which is said to be nine points of the law will
have been guaranteed, and the Market Street Railway
Company will have a hold on Geary street. . between !
THE SAIM FRANCISCO CALL, SATURDAY, MAY 13, 1899.
Grant avenue and Taylor street, from which it cannot
be dislodged. It then proposes, as we are credibly
informed, to surrender the operated Geary street
franchise and to obtain a renewal, under the existing
law, embracing the present line from Market street
to the Park, and extended thence" to the Cliff House.
If this can be accomplished it has been reasonably
estimated that, by the expenditure of three hundred
thousand dollars or less, it can insure a return in
round numbers of not less than 6 per cent per annum
on two million dollars. The grandeur of this con
ception is staggering to the ordinary citizen, but it is
a familiar phase of the Huntington policy, and, after
all, only part of a stupendous whole.
The laborious newspaper attorney in fact for the
railroad, in its advocacy of this intricate project of
gripping the major part of San Francisco with the
sharp and unrelaxing tentacles of the Market street
monopoly, proudly boasts that so- far the fares have
not been increased. The public has, been chiefly in
terested in possible reductions. If the fraud on the
new charter and the general clean-up now before the
Board of Supervisors is accomplished, the shortest
and cheapest mode of consummation would be a con
veyance of all the surviving rights of the municipality.
THE PRESIDENT AND THE ADMIRAL.
SAN FRANCISCO, through properly accredited
representatives, has cordially invited a visit
from President McKinley and from Admiral
Dewey. There awaits a most cordial welcome for
each, should he come to us. We on this coast have
not seen so many dignitaries of national rank as to
have become blase with respect to them, like the peo
ple of the East. When a distinguished man of the
I highest rank comes to us we make a holiday and give
him a reception that becomes a notable event in our
annals.
From the tone of the dispatches from Washington
it seems very unlikely that we shall have the pleasure
of receiving Dewey. The Admiral will probably go
home by way of Suez, land at New York and make his
way to Washington by the shortest route. That choice
is not a surprising one, for it is certain that if Dewey
came to San Francisco and took the tour across the
continent the receptions given him at every city and
way station along the route would be more fatiguing
than the Philippine campaign. The Admiral, accord
ing to all reports, is not in robust health, and he may
have good reason for shrinking from the tumultuous
greetings he would have to face from San Francisco
: to Washington if he accept our invitation.
There is a much better prospect of a visit from the
President. Indeed, that visit seems well assured.
McKinley has long planned a trip to the Pacific
Coast, and the coming summer promises to afford
him an 'excellent opportunity for making it. He is
now at Hot Springs, Virginia, building up his
; strength, which has been sadly worn by the nervous
'â¢ strain of the harassing duties of his high office, and
' as soon as he is fully restored to health he will prob
j ably start West. It would, in fact, be wise on his
I part to come before the heat of the Eastern summer
1 sets in, for there is no better place at which to seek
refuge from the sweltering East than on this coast.
Californians will need no urging to induce them to
make extensive preparations for the President's visit.
As soon as it is assured that he is coming every com
munity along the line of his route in the State will
be eager to welcome him and do him honor. It is
to be hoped he will not undertake a hurried trip.
The experience of President Harrison's hasty tour
some years ago is sufficient to show that such a
! course is unsatisfactory to all concerned. To enjoy
' his visit and to derive the full benefit of the journey
the President should remain at least a month within
the limits of the commonwealth. He will find abun
dant employment for every day of it, and his welcome
will show no diminution of popular enthusiasm.
There are many reasons why Californians should
be glad to receive McKinley. With his inauguration
there began that revival of prosperity which is now
so abundant throughout the Union. He stands as the
! foremost representative and champion of that states
manship which upbuilds American industries, and
every class of citizens, from capitalists to working
men, will unite to do him honor.
PHILANTHROPY AND SENSE.
ANDREW CARNEGIE has given an explicit j
and emphatic denial to the report that his ac- j
tion in closing out his business interests was I
due to a dislike of trusts. He declares he is neither ,
antagonistic to trusts nor is afraid of them. The ;
Carnegie Steel Company, he asserts, is the greatest
property of the kind in the world' and has a prosper
ous future as well assured as anything can be in i
human affairs. He has retired from business, he says, I
simply because he desires to live a dignified and un- \
selfish life, freed from all struggling after more
dollars.
. He has given no intimation of how he proposes to
enjoy the leisure he has so well earned, nor of what
he will do with his fortune, which has been estimated
at the enormous sum of $100,000,000. When his atten
tion was called to an editorial in a London paper re
ferring to his noted statement of some years ago, "To
die rich is to die disgraced," he is reported to have ;
said: "Tell that editor to watch and see. I hope j
I shall not justify the definition, sometimes deserved, I
of a philanthropist as a man with a great deal of I
money and very Tittle sense.'"
. There was a time, when the word "philanthropist" j
was used to signify one who so loved his fellowman !
that he devoted the best energies of his life to the ser
vice of humanity. John Howard, Samuel Wilber
force, Elizabeth Fry and others like persons of j
little wealth but rich in zeal and animated with a j
genuine sympathy for all who sufferâ -were alone
honored with that title, but now it has come to mean
a rich man of many millions who makes a gift to the
public.
As a rule the greater portion of the wealth distrib
! uted by successful Americans has been well directed.
! Few among them have posed as philanthropists.
Many have expressly disclaimed the title. They have
', given of their fortunes for the foundation of schools,,
! colleges, libraries, art galleries and charitable institu
; tions. In nearly all such instances their liberality has
: been widely beneficial.. It happens, however, that
some ostentatious givers have bestowed their gifts
from considerations of vanity or sentiment, and as
many of these have been harmful rather than helpful
to the communities receiving them, they have given
; rise to the definition to which Carnegie refers.
It is indisputable that a rich man without sense is
i more injurious than a rich man without munificence.
Wealth is rarely ever better employed than when in
vested in great industries that give work and wages
to thousands of people. To withdraw it from that
employment for the purpose of bestowing it upon
promiscuous charities is by no means an act of good
; sense. â  Mr, Carnegie evidently means he is not going
Jto scatter his wealth in that way. He is now a very
; interesting figure to all institutions in America and
! Great Britain that would like endowments, and he
j need not fear but what he will be watched by more
; neoole than the editor whose watchine he has invited.

TIME FOR ITS BECOMING OPERA
TIVE EXPRESSLY FIXED.
*
Lawyers Undertake to Recover the
Amounts Paid for Licenses
Since February on a Con
â¢ tingent.
Some of the heaviest license-payers in
| the city, including merchants, bankers
and theatrical managers, have signed a
petition, at the instance of Knight &
Heggerty and Frank McGowan, lawyers,
demanding the refunding of the amounts
they have paiu into the License Collect
or's office since February, on the ground
that the new charter abolishes' the office
of License Collector and that the charter
became operative immediately upon its
approval by the Legislature.
These business men have signed the pe
tition with the understanding that they
will receive 50 per cent of the amount so
refunded and shall not be put to any ex
pense for the collection, the attorneys de
fraying all costs and taking the other 50
per cent for their trouble. It is asserted
that fully 1500 license-payers have entered
into the agreement with the lawyers.
It is not apparent how the lawyers ex
pect to prove their contention, for the
charter Itself expressly provides that it
shall become operative on January 1, 1900.
The following is taken from an anno
tated volume on the new charter by At
torney E. F. Treadwell:
When the charter takes eft-actâ By the pro
visions of this schedule the charter, except for
certain purposes, goes into effect on the Ist
day of January. IWO. . Section s of article XI
of the constitution provides that if the charter
is "approved by a majority vote of the mem
bers elected to each house, it shall become the
charter of such city," etc. Under the pro
vision there might be some â¢ question as to
wfcether the charter does not take effect as
SGWi as adopted by the legislature; but It is
not believed that this is the effect of the pro
visions of the constitution.
This question was first raised by Tax
Collector Sheehan early in February,
when he applied to the .Supervisors fur
more funds with which to conduct his of
fice, claiming that under the new charter
the duties of License Collector had de
volved upon him, and as that instrument
had gone into effect immediately upon its
approval by the Legislature, it was nec
essary for him to employ additional help
to collect licenses.
The matter was referred to City and
County Attorney Lane, who submitted a
written opinion to the board on Febru
ary 18 adverse to the Tax Collector's
â¢ contention. Attorney Lane quoted at
length from the State constitution in sup
port of his 'finding. He said there was
nothing in the constitution in conflict
with the express provision of the charter
postponing the time of its becoming ope
rative until January 1, 1900. It might go
into effect immediately upon its approval
by the Legislature if the charter itself
did not' fix the date upon which it was
to become operative. In closing Attorney
Lane said: "I therefore advise that the
new charter is not now in operation, *ââ¢
Cept for the purpose of an election to be
held in November next, and, further, that
for general purposes it does not go into
effect until January 1, 1900."
That, apparently, ls about as good law
as any the attorneys who are seeking to
mulct the city out of many thousands of
dollars may have to support their conten
tion, but it is possible they may have a
joker up their sleeve. If they have, they
are careful not to expose it now. Frank
McGowan was not inclined to discuss the
matter yesterday beyond stating that a
test would be made shortly.
But even this test. it appears. is not to
be made directly on the question of li
censes. The plan as at present outlined
is to induce some employe of the city,
who is entitled to more pay under the
charter, to institute mandamus proceed
ings against Auditor Asa R. wells to
compel him to audit his claim at the in
creased rate. Efforts were made to in
duce some captain of police to begin suit
on these lines, but there is an evident
shyness among those interested to do
this but it is hoped that somebody will
be found who will be willing to have the
fight made in his name.
Prominent lawyers take little stock in
the contention, and"even the business men
themselves who ' have gone into the
scheme do not express much hope of get
ting any returns from it. As one of them
â¢said yesterday: "It doesn't cost us any
thing to attempt to get our license money
back and if we receive 50 per cent we
are just that much ahead. The lawyers
take all the risk and they are welcome to
the other half." .

The intent of the resolution recently
I adopted by the Board of Supervisors rel
ative to the preparation of plans for a
i sewer system to be submitted to the peo
! ple at a bond election bids fair to be
frustrated by reason of the alleged In
\ eligibility of one of the three Commission

terms of the resolution the work was del
egated to a commission composed of
Marsden Manson, C. E. Grunsky and
City and County Surveyor Tilton. Each
jof the Commissioners is to receive the
i sum of $4000.
It is this item of compensation that is
! causing all of the trouble, as it is al
! leged that under the law the Board of
Supervisors has no power to grant addi
. tional compensation to an official who
: receives a salary from the municipal gov
'; ernment. It is also asserted that the
â  same law debars a salaried official from
receiving or accepting any remuneration
from the city other than his salary. If
this construction of the law is correct
Mr. Tilton's membership in the commis
; sion will have to be canceled and the va
, cancy will have to be filled by resolution
;of the Board of Supervisors. The time ,
! required to correct this mistake, if one !
i has been made, will so retard the inaug
uration and completion of the work re
quired of the commission that not suffi- i
, cient time will be left to comply with i
the provisions of the law in the matter
iof calling a bond election. The election
must be held during the present year if |
iat all. Messrs. Manson and Grunsky es
timate that it will require at least four j
months' time to make the necessary sur- j
j veys and prepare the plans for the pro-
I posed sewer system. This being so, the j
; delay of a month in the inauguration of
the work will prove fatal to the plan for
holding a bond election in. December.

ty Surveyor Tilton is not eligible to a
place on the commission at a salary of

THE FIRST POSTOFFICEâ M.. City.
The first postofflce in San Francisco was
established in 1849 at the corner of Clay
and Pike (now Wavefly place) streets. It
was subsequently moved to Brenham
place and Clay street, then to the -south
side of Clay street, near Kearny, and then
to the building on the corner of Washing
ton and Battery streets, where it now ls.
MARRIAGE AND INSANITYâ W. D.
M., Comptche, Cal. Insanity is not one of
the grounds upon which a husband can
apply for a divorce in California, that is,
If insanity developed after marriage, but
a marriage may be annulled if insanity
existed at the time of marriage, unless
such party after coming to reason freely
cohabit together as husband and wife.
In such case the action may be com
menced by the party injured or relative
or guardian of the party of unsound mind
at any time before the death of either
party.

Robert Cleary, 1658 Market.

The above-named gentlemen, who qual
ified as talesmen on the jury of Peter Mc-
Glade, late of the Street Department,
who was charged with forging various
warrants upon the City and County
Treasurer, came into court late yesterday
afternoon and filed a verdict finding th*
defendant not guilty of the crime charged
against him. The verdict was as great a
surprise to the accused as it was to the
court-frequenters, but the end is not yet.
McGlade must stand trial upon a score
more of forged warrants before he can
call himself a free man, and the term
necessary to try these cases stretches
itself out along a weary length; so doubt
less the decision of yesterday failed to
materially raise the hopes of the accused.
McGlade was tried upon a specific
charge of having forged a warrant upon
the Street Department for $96 in the
name of a party who "was not only a
minus quantity upon the payroll of the
Street Department but as well on any
branch of the municipal government. Mc-
Glade's counsel contended that inasmuch
as the warrant was illegal upon Its face
it was not the subject of a forgery. After
a lengthy argument Judge Cook over
ruled the objection of the defendant's
counsel to the admission of the docu
ment and the case proceeded. A majority
of the officials connected with the Super
intendent of Streets' office were called to
testify against the accused, and hand
writing experts gave their opinion ...at
the document upon which the charge was
based was forged by the accused. In de
fense of the charge McGlade was placed
upon the stand and his testimony alone
-was presented, after which the case was
argued. â¢ Shortly before noon the jury was
charged and retired to deliberate upon a
verdict. The hours passed and no signal
from the jury called the Deputy Sheriff
to the door. Just as the time for an ad
journment came the bell signaled that a
verdict had been reached. Judge Cook
was summoned, the jury, was brought into
court and announced that the defendant
was not guilty. Congratulations were
then in order, but McGlade was not ex
tremely cheerful, as other charges yet re
main to be heard. Judge Cook dis
missed the jury that freed the accused,
and put the cases still standing against
the accused over for further consid
eration. The courtroom was then cleared,
McGlade held a whispered conversation
with his counsel and the day's session
was at an end. â¢ â â :-' \- ".â "â¢â ,

Housekeeper.
Judge Mogan found yesterday that Mrs.
Amanda J. Fiske-Marceau-Fennell was
:he victim of a most "unfortunate mis
take," which is the way the gallant ex
ponent of the law put the <as of embez
zlement and larceny preferred against the

Mrs. Marceau had been occupying the
furnished Levy flat at 2112 Devisadero
street, and while moving the Japanese
servants had unwittingly packed the land-
lord's belongings with those of their mis- i
tress. When Levy discovered his loss he
at once secured a search warrant, and ac- \
companied by an officer proceeded to the
present Marceau residence at 834 Haight
street. Here he discovered the greater
part of the missing articles, which Mrs.
Marceau gladly and immediately restored
to him.
Yesterday morning in Judge Mogan's
court Levy explained in detail the exact
number of sheets, pillow-cases, table
cloths and napkins that were still coming
to him. and also made reference to a set
of carvers, likewise a rose Jar, which was
masquerading in the inventory as bric-a
brac Levy's testimony failed to make
out any intent on the part of the fair de
fendant to steal.
Mrs. Mar' took the stand in her own
defense, and while -plaining how Levy's
things got packed up with her own inci
dentally lei the court Into many house
hold secrets and bits of entertaining per
sonal gossip. Mrs. Marceau was gowned

In the very latest fashion and leaned back

was a confidential, conversational one, and
she tactfully overruled all Interruptions

narrative Judge Mogan decided that there
was no evidence of criminal Intent, a.nd

AN ENERGETIC PASTOR
Rev. W. B. Anderson Hopes to Re-
duce His Church's Debt.
Rev. W. B. Anderson, pastor of Bethel
A. M. E. Church, Powell street, near
Jackson, has the membership of his
church well organized. They have been
on a financial campaign for several weeks
trying by church entertainments and so
licitations to get $1200 together by to-mor
row, the 14th. Their church property has
a mortgage of $6500 upon it, but this the
pastor, who enjoys the highest esteem of
his Bock, together with them, has worked
nobly during the last three years to free
the church from debt. It Was thought at
one time before Rev. Mr. Anderson took
charge that the church would be lost, but
since he has had charge and proved to be
the right sort of leader all fear of such
j a catastrophe is banished. The church !
i was almost $9000 ln debt three years ago; ;
j now its Indebtedness is $6500.
The rally that takes place to-morrow i
â  promises to surpass any previous gather
j ing. The congregation is deserving of
! public help and the people will doubtless
; turn out and assist materially In relieving
this burdened church.
Following is the programme: 6 a. m
prayer meeting, led by the pastor; 11 a.
m., sermon by Rev. John Pointer, P. E ;
1:30 p. m., rally by the Sunday-school, j
H. Fisher, superintendent; 3 p. m., ad
dresses by Rev. E. T. Cottman of Oak
; land and Rev. T. Brown of Zion's Church;
7:4.", p. m.. Rev. J. li. Kelly of the Third
Baptist Church and Rev. T. Brown of
Zlon's Church will have charge; Rev
Kelly will -reach the sermon and Rev!
Mr. Brown will make the appeal for the
collection. At this service all of the dif
ferent clubs will make their report, and it '
i is quite assured that the $1200 asked for
| will ice realized.
Her Application Denied.
Judge Hunt has dismissed the second
petition of Mrs. Clara Augusta Gordon
Wilson for partial distribution to her and
her four children of a portion of the estate
of the late John C. Wilson, the capitalist,
who died in 1897. Wilson left behind him
a will In which he bequeathed the bulk of
an estate valued at $200,000 to his widow,
Agnes Wilson, and the residue to two
sons. John C. and Hugh Wilson.
Eighteen months* afterward Mrs. Clara
Augusta Gordon Wilson, claiming to be a
widow, of the deceased, petitioned the
court for a partial distribution of the
estate and asked that decedent's will be
revoked and set aside. Judge Hunt yes
terday decided that the application for a
partial distribution of the estate â  was
nothing but an application to revoke the
will, which was barred by the statute of
limitations. .Hence he dismissed the ap
plication, and the estate will be distrib
uted according to the will heretofore filed.

THE SAN FRAN CISCO CALL, SATURDAY, MAY 13, 1899.
THREE PEOPLE CAUGHT
IN SIDEWALK ELEVATORS
JA.MES ALLIATO. a workman in
the employment of P. H. Jackson
& Co. at 228 First street, met with
a terrible death yesterday morn
ing in the sidewalk elevator on the
premises of the Remington Arms
Company at 425 Market street. He had
been sent by his employers to make
certain repairs and Improvements to
the elevator of the Remington Arms
Company building, and having occa
sion to open the Iron doors in the side
walk he started on the elevator, in
tending to unlock the doors in his up
ward passage and thus emerge from
the basement. But the elevator went
too fast for him, or he lost his pres
ence <*f mind, and before he had a
chance to open the doors he was
crushed against a girder in the side
walk.
The victim was still breathing and
moaning when placed in the ambu
lance, but he died before the vehicle
reached the City Receiving Hospital.
The body was taken to the Morgue
and an inquest will be held.
Alllato was unmarried, a native of
Switzerland, 35 years old.
Marion Cohen, a cash boy in Bea
mish's store. 91S Market street, was
seriously injured in a sidewalk ele
vator yesterday afternoon, both bones of his left leg being fractured. The
boy was sent to the basement to wait till the scavengers had taken away
the refuse. After they left he went Into the elevator, locked the door and
took hold*Of the? rope to start It. He pulled on the rope too hard and the
elevator gave a sudden jerk that caused the boy^S head to strike against
the top and he fell. His left leg was jammed between the wall and the bot
tom of the elevator.
Cohen's cries for help were heard by several of the employes in the store *
and they ran to his assistance. As the door was locked It took some time *
and ingenuity to get the boy out of his painful predicament, and as soon as *
lie was released he fainted. He was taken to the Receiving Hospital, where .
it was found that he had sustained a compound fracture of both-t>ones of the â 
left leg. He lives at 1617 Church street. *
About the same time John McGrath, a boy employed in the delivery de- *
partment In Hale Bros." store on Market street and who lives at 1620 Church *
â¢street, was taken to the hospital with lacerated wounds of the forehead *
and eyebrows. He went to the New York Kitchen, 915 Market street, to col- *
lect a bill, and thought he would go to the basement in the chute used for
lowering boxes. When he got Inside the wind blew the door on him and he
was struck on the head and knocked senseless. *

At the special session of the Board of
Regents yesterday afternoon the election
of a new president of the State Uni
versity was deferred until the regular
meeting in June. The regents attending
the meeting yesterday were: T. G. Phelps
(presiding), James A. Waymire, Dr.
George C. Pardee, A. B. Spreckels, Alden
Anderson. Thomas J. Kirk, Charles \V.
Slack, Arthur Rodgers, Dr. Chester Row
ell, General Houghton. John E. Budd,
E. A. Denlcke. A. S. Hallldie, H. 6. Foote,
William T. Wallace. Albert Miller and
President Kellogg.
According to the estimates of tne
finance committee the additional sum of
$47,000 is needed to maintain the Institu
tion for the next year. A proposition to
impose a tax on the Students to raise the
amount required provoked a vigorous re
sistance. â¢
Judge Wallace contended that it would
be contrary to law to Impose the tax. It
would be a shook to the public sentiment
of the State if the regents should do this
thing. He spoke logically and earnestly
against the proposed measure of taxa
tion.
Dr. Rowell of Fresno registered a pro
test against the proposed tax. He be
lieved the exigencies of the case could be
met by other plans. He was in favor of
revising the budget and reducing ex
penses.
The proposition to tax the students was
defeated. The regents who voted to im
pose the tax were: Denicke, Kellogg,
Hallldie, Houghton, Miller and Phelps.
In further discussion respecting means
JAMES ALLIATO.
for adjusting the financial difficulties the
statement was made by Regent Budd
that salaries had been increased $100,000 '
in the past three years.
Judge . Waymire believed that th* re- '
gents could safely count on receiving
next year more money from the 2 cent
tax than was secured this year
Mr. Hallldie gave notice that he would
not vote to elect a new president until
the financial affairs of the institution
were settled.
Judge Slack believed in having a frank
talk with the heads of departments at
Berkeley. He called to mind that financial ;
distress in the past had been tided over
without recourse to a tax on students
The suggestion was made that $7700 ex
pended on a fad. known as pedagogy or
teaching teachers how to teach, might be
saved. No one suggested bringing a suit
to recover $2400 paid for lobbying at Sac
ramento two years ago.
Regents Rowell. Slack and Denicke
were appointed to assist the finance com
mittee In devising a plan to overcome the
financial obstacles now confronting the !
university. The committee, was In
structed to submit a report at the June" :
meeting.
When Regent Rodgers observed tnat
his associates on the board, and particu
larly Hallldie and Miller, were in no
frame of mind promising unanimity in
the selection of a president, he moved
that the subject be postponed until the
June meeting. y>, . â¢
Mr. Hallldie handed to the secretary
the list of names submitted or recom
mended. He said the men whose names
were on the list were not candidates for
the presidency. They were not seeking
the office, but had been recommended.
At the suggestion of one of the regents,
the secretary began to read the names.
He got no further along than the first
nameâ that of J. E. Stubbs, president of
the Nevada State Universityâ before pro
tests were entered against giving the â 
names to the public.
Regents Wallace and Foote wanted to
know what good could be served by with
holding the names from the public.
Mr. Rodgers gave an example of the
evil effects of publicity. He said, for in
stance, if D. C. Oilman's name should be !
on the list and the list made public it
might place Mr. Oilman in a delicate posi- I
tion before his trustees. The same might
be said of President Adams of the Wis
consin University. The convincing and
unanswerable arguments of Regent Rod- :
gers had so much weight that the board
decided to give the list of names to the j
regents but not to the press.
The list in question appears at the head :
of this report of the special meeting, but
It should be considered that it is there for '
! the convenience of regents and not for i
public enlightenment.

THE PACIFIC COAST BISCUIT COMPANY owns and comprises the follow-
ing well-known long-established and profitable companies: The American Biscuit
Company. San Francisco, Cal.; Portland Cracker Company, Portland, Or. ; Wash-
ington Cracker Company, Spokane, Wash.; Seattle Cracker and Candy Company,
Seattle. Wash.: Oregon Cracker Company, Portland, Or.; Southern California
Cracker Company, Los Angeles, Cal., and the Standard Biscuit Company, San
Francisco. Cal.
All of the properties are valuable. The machinery and equipment are modern
and of -high degree of efficiency. The properties are free of all debts and in-
cumbrances and the new corporation starts with ample working capital.
The product is a staple necessity. The industry is an Important and growing
one and is not affected by the tariff or financial disturbances.
The management, as can readily be seen, is strong, experienced, capable
and Conservative.
A thorough examination of these properties has been made by Mr. Robert
Shaw of Chicago, who has been for many years in the Biscuit Business, who
certifies that they are valuable and well adapted to the business. An expert
accountant engaged by the organizers of the new corporation certifies, after a
thorough examination of the accounts for a period of years, that the earnings of
the various companies operated separately have made for a number of years
past, In spite of the severe competition which has existed among them, a net
average annual profit of $205,000 per annum and that a conservative estimate of
the earnings of the company after consolidation would be in excess of $400,000
without raising the price of standard goods to the Consumer.
The PREFERRED STOCK of $100 per share is cumulative, and dividends of
7 per cent are payable semi-annually. It has full voting power and together with
the common stock is registered and is issued full paid non-assessable, carrying
no personal liability.
The GOLD BONDS of $1000 each .are a first mortgage on the land, buildings
and machinery of the Corporation. Interest 6 % per annum, payable' May and
November. Dated May Ist, 1899, and run for 20 years, with privilege of being
redeemed after 10 years at 105. A sinking fund of $25,000 per annum begins
three years from date of organization, to be set aside before payment of dividend
on common stock. Bonds may be registered. *
The COMMON STOCK has been entirely subscribed for by those directly in-
terested in the management.
Application will be made to list the Bonds and Stock on the New York and San
Francisco Exchanges. /'

POLICE WANT A PATROL BOAT.
Members of the Board of Health ap
peared before the Health and Police Com
mittee of the Board of Supervisors yes
terday and presented reasons why the
State quarantine boat should not be taken
away from the Health Department and
turned over to the Police Department for
use in patrolling the water front. Dr.
Williamson and others stated that the
State laws require that the State Quaran
tine Officer shall board and inspect all
Incoming vessels and that It would be im
possible to perform, this duty as required
by statute should the quarantine boat be
turned* over to the Police Department.
He stated that the inefficiency of the
Federal quarantine service made it im
perative that the present system of quar
; antine be continued in order to protect
' the city against the introduction and
spread of contagious diseases.
Chief Lees, on behalf of the Police De
partment, said that he was not particular
whether he was given the use of the pres
â  ent quarantine boat or some other equally
serviceable vessel, but that the necessi
ties of his department demanded a boat,
and he looked to the Board of Supervisors
to provide him with one. The matter was
taken under advisement by the com- j
mittee. I
The committee considered Informally
the proposition of the San Francisco Ath- j
letic Club to hold a boxing contest In the I
near future. The club has not applied
for and has not been granted a permit to
hold the fight which is now being adver
j tised on all of the downtown street cor
ners nor has It paid the necessary license
fee. ' Chief Lees assured the members of
the committee that unless the club com
plied with all of the requirements of the
law he would not permit the exhibition
1 to be held.

COMMERCIAL WORLD.
SUMMARY OF THE MARKETS.
Silver lower.
Wheat and Barley dull.
Oats. Corn and Rye unchanged.
Hay lower and demoralized.
Considerable change in Deans.
Potatoes steady and Onions weak.
Vegetables about the same.
Larger supplies of Butter.
cheese lower. Eggs steady.
Poultry about the same.
Larger assortment of Fresh Fruits.
Heavy purchases of Prunes.
Hams continue active and firm.
Veal scarce and higher.
Hogs firm. Beef and Mutton unchanged.
Decreased exports of Produce.
Large Increase in Mint coinage.

WEATHER CONDITIONS AND GENERAL
FORECAST.
The area of high pressure central Friday
morning over the northwest coast has moved
steadily inland. The pressure has fallen slow
ly along the entire Pacific slope, but no well
marked depression is to be noted.
The temperature has fallen about * degrees
in the great valleys of California, but will rise
to-mom>w. Generally warmer weather with
high maximum temperatures in the interior
may be expected Saturday and Sunday. The
maximum temperatures to-day did not exceed
jeci degrees, except at Yuma, where 102 degrees
is reported. No rain has fallen In California.
A maximum wine! velocity of 28 miles per hour
from the south ls reported at Idaho Falls,
Forecast made at San Francisco for thirty
hours ending midnight. May 13;
Northern Californiaâ and warmer Sat
urday; northerly winds.
Southern Californiaâ Fair and warmer Sat- j
urday night and Sunday; north winds.
Nevadaâ Fair Saturday.
Utahâ Fair Saturday.
Arizonaâ Fair Saturday.
San Francisco and vicinity Fair Saturday;
warmer; north, changing to west wind.
ALEXANDER McADIE.
Forecast Official.
EASTERN MARKETS.
NEW YORK STOCK MARKET.
NEW YORK, May 12â The bulls had the
better of the speculative contest to-day, but
the edge was taken off the rise in the last
few minutes of trading by a vigorous bear
raid. The continued purchases for London ac
count, and the. lack of strain in either the
money or sterling exchange markets were
features of confidence. Moreover, It was an
nounced that ' the final $5,000,000 warrant in
connection with the Spanish indemnity would
he deposited in a local bank, which announced
that all the needed exchange had been secured,
and this was favorably Interpreted as to its
probable effect on sterling exchange. The
known movements of money, although often
misleading, were thought to forecast a favor
able bank statement, as there had been but
few known Industrial consolidations financed.
At times the stock market was very erratic.
There was first a recession from the opening
high level, and then a gradual recovery until
near the close. Sugar moved over a radius
of nearly ten points and closed with a rise
of six; Brooklyn Transit lost nearly a point
net. but closed nearly two points above the
figures reached in the raid. American Tobacco
lost nearly three points after a dip of twice
as much. Metropolitan dropped to 218%, jumped
over six "points and left off with a gain of
over two points. The Iron and steel shares
were rather weak, as were the coalers, but
the Pacifies and grangers were strong with
fractional advances appearing pretty generally
ln the rest.
Immense blocks of high grade mortgages were
taken to-day at Improved prices and the
strength of these issues exercised a stimulat
ing effect on the speculative liens. Consider
able activity was noted The market generally
Improved. Total sales $4,530,000.
United States new fours and the old fours
coupon advanced %, nnd do registered and the
threes % in the bid price. '
The total sales of stocks to-day amounted
to 629,380 shares, including: American Hoop,

THE LONDON MARKET.
NEW YORK. May 12.â Commercial Ad
vertiser's London financial cablegram says:
The markets here were less active and less
strong to-day. Americans opened hard, coun
try buyers closing their accounts and there
being some fresh London buying. New York
accepted the rise for an hour, then the market
slipped away to the worst. Enthusiasm Is
evaporating here. Berlin is still buying Can
adian Pacific. Ttntos were 48%@49%; Ana
condas. 13 6-16; Utahs, 8%; Boston, 2%.
Money was stiff and on the settlement there
was a little borrowing at the hank. Silver
was dull. There was no pressure to sell, but
absolute lack of demand.
The Chancellor of the Exchequer has reduced
the new stamp duty on American certificates
from 5, to 1 shilling. The Japanese loan of
Â£10,000,000 is being underwritten and will prob
ably be offered at 90 in a few days.

BRADSTREET'S FINANCIAL REVIEW.
NEW YORK, May 12.â Bradstreet's financial
review to-morrow will say:
At the beginning of the week the market ex
perience.] a general decline, attended by con
siderable liquidation of long stock. On Wednes
day it rallied sharply on support by large in
terests and exceptionally large purchases for
London. This fact was. however, apparently
due to the existence- of a large interest in
Americans in the foreign market, while cov
ering by the local professional bears also con
tributed toward the recovery of prices. The
European purchasing continued on Thursday,
and there was also further evidence of support
from the so-called big people, but the market
developed hesitation and declining tendencies,
Its weakness centering in the stocks which
would be affected should the Ford franchise
tax law become cine of the laws of New York.
On Friday the action of speculation was Ir
regular. An early decline was checked by the
strength of Sugar and other manipulated
stocks, and the expectation of a good bank
statement also had an effect.
It is noticed that the drop In prices this week
was in the main the result of a purely specu
lative case, to wit, the Overbought condition
of the market. Stocks have accumulated ln
the hands of people who bought on margin in
expectation of continued rise The buying
power has, however, become measurably ex
hausted, while disappointment at the action of
the market produced as it would naturally a
disposition to realize. The decline of last week.
though not severe, aggravated this tendency,
while in many cases margins were weakened to
an extent which furnished occasion for en
forced liquidation. One of the most prominent
manipulative ooerators of the street, whose
name was by common report coupled with a
supposed bear syndicate, denies the impeach
ment. But other professional speculators cer
tainly took an aggressive stand on the bear
side and contributed by the selling pressure
they produced to bring about the outpouring of
long stocks which characterized the mark-is
on Monday and Tuesday. Other conditions.
however, played their separate parts in the
movement. The expectation of an unfavorable
Government crop report was considered, though
the street apparently disregarded on Wednes
day, and it was not until Thursday that the
Grager stocks were sold on the theory that the
report shows a reduction of the whole output
calculated' to affect railroad traffic and earn
ings. The market has not suffered from any
fears of tight money. On the contrary, sup
plies of funds seem to lie Increasing and the
rates were appreciably easier. The collection
from the Government of 815.000,000 of the Span
ish indemnity by the Institution which acted
In the matter of remitting the 820,000,000 was a
factor, and the movement of money now seems
to be toward the metropolis. The bond mar
ket has been unaffected by the events affect
ing speculative values, and the demand for in
vestments ls as large and almost as eager as
at any previous time. On the other hand the
financial public has finally begun to appreciate
the effects of the continued creation of new In
dustrials securities, and a conservative senti
ment has asserted Itself In this connection. It
â was generally remarked that thÂ«Â» alleged propo
sition to erect a billion-dollar steel trust by a
combination of the industrials also formed In
that trade seemed to touch the limits of the in
dustrial movement, and some relief was felt
when it was known that the foundation for
this was merely the nlan to consolidate the
Carnegie properties and to purchase the inter
ests of their founder In them. The appetite of
the public for industrials has not. however, de
parted, as shown by the J412.000.000 subscription
to the $75,000,000 Stock of the amalgamated cop
per company, although that enterprise has
been severely criticized in some quarters, and it
is noted that the stock rose quickly from a pre
mium of about five to one of a fraction above
par.
BRADSTREET'S ON TRADE CONDITION?.
NEW YORK. May 12.â Bradstreet's to-mor
row will say: Reports as to trade conditions
are generally of a favorable tenor, notable
among these being advanced quotations for
many grades of iron and steel, increased ac
tivity in building lines reflected In heavy sales
of lumber at prices tending upward, a firmer
tone In the cotton goods market, sympathized
in by the raw product, bank clearings sur
passing all records for this week of the year,
railroad earnings maintaining recent gains and
business failures, as for some time past, down
to the minimum. Offsetting these in some
measure might be cited the seasonable quiet
ing down of wholesale distributing demand,
particularly for dry goods and clothing, but
most notable of all, the further weakening of
cereal values, reflecting the indifference of for
eign buyers, in spite of the definitely con
ceded fact that our winter wheat acreage has
been considerably curtailed, lower quotations
of hog products and some shading of prices In
sugar, copper and tin. If conditions of de
mand, supply, invisible wheat reserved and
foreign crop advices did not favor lower prices
the Government report might have been re
garded as a bullish publication. The weak
ness of the market, under the circumstances
may, therefore, be regarded as reflecting the
feeling among foreign consumers that good
crops abroad and the refilling of deplete, 1 re
serves from last year's yield will furnish
wheat In plenty.
The demand ln iron and steel does not weak
en. Production of plglron, though at a mln
THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, SATURDAY, MAY 13, 1599.
imum, is still inadequate, and the stocks of
plglron show a further decrease.' Reports that
possible foreign business in this line has been
rejected come from producing centers, but the
efforts of foreigners to obtain supplies here is,
nevertheless, testimony to their own productive
abilities being heavily taxed. It seems evi
dent that the first real inspiration In the wool
industry must come from abroad. Heavy buy
ing of Australian grades for re-export has
been again the feature, but coupled with this
are reported liberal sales at leading markets
of domestic wool to go abroad.
In the building trades, activity is very gen- j
erally reported, sales of lumber are large and I
the strong statistical position of nearly all j
grades foreshadows continued firmness or a |
further advance. Hides and leather are In 1
good demand for raw, while the manufactured
product is moving well at recent advances.

els last week, 3.646,542 bushels ln correspond
ing season of 1898, 2.183,875 bushels In 1897.

DUN'S WEEKLY REVIEW OF TRADE.
NEW YORK, May 12.â G. Dun & Co. in
j their weekly review of trade will say:
To speak truly of business this week one
; must free the mind of stocks, for a severe
decline in manufacturing stocks has created
j an impression that It has some relation to I
productive industry, but it has none. There !
was no change in the money market worth !
I mentioning and the volume of business shown I
by payments through the principal clearing-
.uses was 71.5 per cent larger than, in 1892, '
the year > -i greatest prosperity hitherto, which l
ls more than double the gain anybody con
sidered possible a year ago.
Never before reaching a quarter of a mil
lion tons per week, the Iron Manufacturer
reports May 1, an output of 250,093 tons weekly,
which, with 54,455 decrease In warrant and
furnace stocks, indicates a consumption of
37,322 tons daily, against 37.035 In March, 34,814 1
in February and 36,834 In January. The aver- 1
age for four months Is 38,027 tons dally, against !
32,209 last year. An increase of 16 per cent I
in the- year would P.- less interesting if con
sumption a year ago were not far beyond any
thing previously realized. Yet proposed com- I
binations hinder large orders In bars and
sheets, while the structural business is as ;
heavy as ever in small lots, with bid contracts
about to come at Chicago for the drainage
canal and the plate business too big for all I
the works to handle, invites no consolidation,
the Chicago and Philadelphia works being be
hind in deliveries and Pittsburg works troubled !
by demands for 4000 tons of vessel plates and !
4000 tons more close ahead for the Government ,
docks at Algiers.
Only pig Iron advanced, Bessemer 50c and |
grey forge 25c at Pittsburg and local pig 50c
lat Chicago. In minor metals tin declined at
London to 25.23 c and copper is weak at 19c
lor spot and 19% C for futures.
Hides at Chicago rose about 10 per cent, with I
actual receipts of cattle in four months of j
100,000 head loss than last year, and over 200. 1
smaller than in 1533-94, notwithstanding the
gain In population.
Nobody who has studied statistics can tell
why purchases or a million pounds Australian
wool for export this week, following others in !
previous weeks, should advance prices of wool, ;
; nor were purchases of five million pounds of j
i territory wool by a speculator readily explain- |
able. But it is just the time for growers to
ask all they can hope to get. and just when j
the largest manufacturers were ready to buy ;
1 only the smaller are buying. Prices Â»ââ¢ said I
' to have been generally advanced, with higher !
demands from the interior holders. But it re- !
mains a question whether sales of 10,499,000
pounds for the week against 5,269.550 Increase
over 1592 and only a little more in 1597 will
prove profitable. The market for woolen goods, ;
tn waiting for evidences of demand which do I
not yet appear, and the delay in getting the
pew combination into working order causes
some hesitation.
If Government estimates were considered re- ,
liable one based on entirely unknown acreage
of wheat and cotton would be everywhere re
sented. Thus, with official returns pointing :
to a great decrease in winter wheat produc- j
! tion, the price dropped 1 cent and cotton de- j
1 clined for options, though retaining the ad- ]
vance of a sixteenth for spot which was es-
I tabllshed before the Government report ap
j peared Nobody can base a sensible estimate â 
I on a statement of condition which takes no
I account of the acreage of winter wheat killed
i and abandoned. The best that can be said Is
i that worse returns were expected in both ,
' markets, so that the Government report had
j practically no Influence.
Failures for the week have been lfa in the
United States against 246 last year, and 20 in
j Canada against 21 last year.

CHICAGO GRAIN MARKET.
' CHICAGO, May 12.â Unexpectedly firm cables,
foreign covering nnd light domestic receipts
gave wheat a steady start. There was a mo
, mentary weak spell, caused by profit taking,
but after this the market quickly recovered
and ruled strong the remainder of the ses
sion The reported presence of the Hessian
fly and chinch bugs throughout the Middle
West and Southwest encouraged outside buy-
I ing and forced local shorts to cover. Reports
of relief in the congestion at Buffalo and pre
â  dictions of frosts .in the Northwest, where seed
ing has been seriously delayed, helped the ad
vance. July opened unchanged to %c higher at
70Â®70%C advanced to 70% c, declined to 69% c,
rose to 70%Â©70% C and closed with sellers at
70%@70%c.
There was a sharp advance in corn, owing
to small offerings, light receipts, excellent cash
demand and influential country buying. July
closed %c higher.
Oats followed corn. The cash demand was
good, July advanced %c.
The strength in coarse grains and light re
ceipts of bogs steadied prices of provisions.
The effect was only trifling, as there was suffi
cient liquidation to supply the demand from
shorts and packers. Pork and lard advanced
2%c each and ribs sc.
The leading futures ranged as follows:

CALIFORNIA DRIED FRUITS.
NEW YORK, May 12.â The Journal of Com
merce says: The spot supply of California
dried fruits is reported to be smaller than It
has been for several years at this season, and j
it is in few hands. For some time past New j
York has been the chief source of supply for
i dried fruits for markets east of the Rocky
! Mountains, and many orders lor prunes, apri-
I cots and peaches have been placed here within
the past month or six weeks by large buyers
In Chicago, St. Louis, Pittsburg and Philadel
phia Of late, however, inquiries from these
markets have been comparatively few and at
the. present time the demand for goods from
interior points is light.
Holders here show no disposition to urge
sales. The local demand since the beginning
of the month has been small. Jobbers appear
to be well If not liberally supplied, and as the
consuming demand has been moderate they
have not come Into the market except when
absolutely necessary to fill up breaks in stocks.

DRIED FRUITS, NUTS AND RAISINS.
Eastern buyers have ' lately bought some 150
cars of Prunes In this market, thus reducing
the supply to about 50 cars, which is nothing.
The East evidently thinks that Prunes are go
ing to be good property this year. Late reports
from Oregon indicate a serious shortage in
Italians there, and the California crop will be
much shorter than usual.

COALâ Wellington. $8 per ton: New Welling
ton, $3; Southfleld Wellington. $7 50; Seattle. $6;
Bryant, $6; Coos Bay. $5; Wallsend, $7 50;
Scotch. $8; Cumberland.' $8 50 In bulk and $9 50
in sacks; Pennsylvania Anthracite Egg, $14,
Cannel, $S 50 per ton; Rock Springs and Castle
Gate, $7 60; Coke, $12 per ton in bulk and $14
in sacks.
Harrison's circular says: "During the week
there have been six arrivals of coal from Wash
ington with 16,230 tons; seven from British Co
lumbia, 18,210 tons; three from Oregon, 1590
tons; one from Australia, 2756 tons; total, 38,
--786 tons. This amount fully makes up the de
ficiency of last week and serves to make tne
market easy. The spring weather we are now
enjoying diminishes the consumption very ma
terially for domestic uses, still the demand for
steam does not relax, but finds ready sale nt
full prices. We have had only four deliveries
of foreign coal In six weeks, which cleat.,
shows we are almost totally dependent on our
coast collieries for our fuel supplies. If the
craze for establishing "trusts' existed here, that
now prevails East, there is a profitable open
ing here among our colliery proprietors, who
number so few; and their opportunities have
been most favorable, with the Navy Depart
ment â  lately such liberal purchasers, being
forced to pay asking prices, time being a most
important factor In their deliveries. Freights
from England and Australia are very firm: the
anticipated reduction has not materialized; In
fact. It now looks as If present prices are
nearly bedrock for the year: they unquestion
ably are If the outward freights on grain do not
move upward."

Family Retail Market.
Butter and Eggs sell at last week's prices,
but Cheese is cheaper.
Veal is scarcer than for years and dear.
Pork, too, is scarce. Beef and Mutton show
no change. Poultry stands about the same
as last week. Fish is in better supply again.
With the exception of Strawberries the sum
mer fruits are scarce and dear, Cherries es
pecially so. Several new kinds are added to
the list.
Vegetables are generally cheaper than last
week.

THE STOCK MARKET.
Although business was very good in securi
ties on the morning call there were fewer fluc
tuations than usual, the only one worthy of
note being an advance in Giant Powder to $7S.
The sugar stocks were generally steady.
On the afternoon call Contra Costa Water
declined to $72, and Oceanic to $90, recovering
to $91 50. Hawaiian Commercial declined to
$119 25. Giant Powder advanced to $78 50.
Mining stocks stood about the same. The
telegram from the pump said: "Elevator work
ing steadily. Water in the 1750-foot level winze
is 41 feet 10 Inches below the station. All Im
portant connections made. Have just started
the second elevator. It is working very nicely."
The Hale & Norcross assessment of 10 cents
per share will be delinquent In the boards to
day.
A special meeting of the stockholders of the
East Sierra Nevada Mining Company has been
called for July 17 to vote upon a proposition to
diminish the capital stock from its present
amount of $10,000,000, divided into 100,000 shares
of the par value of $100 each, to $100,000, di
vided into 100,000 shares of the par value of $1
each.
The report from the Standard Consolidated
mine for the week ending May 6 states that
raise No. 2 In the new vein in the 318-fOOt level
has 40 Inches of low grade ore in the top. The
north drift, same vein and level, has 24 Inches
of ledge, which is being badly broken. Raise
No. 1 has 24 inches of fair grade ore In the top.
Raise No. 4 In No. 10 vein, same level, has 16
Inches of fair grade ore. Raise 5 shows 15
inches of fair ore. Raise 7 has 24 Inches of fair
ore. The north drift has a 12-inch vein of no
value. In the stoplng ground low grade ore is
coming from the new vein on the 318-foot level,
fair ore from No. 10 vein, same level, and good
ore from the Maguire ledge on the 528-foot level
and Fortuna vein on the 582-foot level and C.
and D. veins on the 270-foot and 336-foot levels.
At the mill 228 tons of ore were crushed for
the week. Average assay vanner tailings, $11 91.
Concentrates produced. 1% tons; assay value,
$276 29. Plate amalgam produced. 1313 Troy
ounces; value per ounce. $1 90. Tailings plant
No. 1 treated 329% tons of tailings for the week.
Tailings plant No. 2 treated about 100 tons.
The last monthly statement of the Alaska-
Mexican mine reports 13,044 tons ore crushed of
the realizable value of $20,680. and 253 tons
sulphurets, valued at $9070. The ore averages
$2 43 per ton. The gross bullion shipment was
$31,669, and the gross expenses $25,508. Tne
Alaska United mine reports 11,863 tons ore
crushed of a value of $24,500, and 245 tons sul
phurets of a value of $7000. Ore averaged $2 82
per ton. Gross expenses for the month were
$27,865. The bullion shipment was $33,460. Gross
receipts for the month were $40,000, including
some sulphurets previously on hand. The new
300-stamp mill of the Alaska-Treadwell was
started on the 6th.

SUN, MOON AND TIDE.
United States Coast and Geodetic Survey-
Times and Heights of High and Low
Waters at Fort Point, entrance to San
Francisco Bay. Published by official au
thority of the Superintendent.
NOTEâ The high and low waters occur at
the city front (Mission-street wharf) about
twenty-five minutes later than at Fort Point;
the height of tide is the same at both places.

NOTEâ In the above exposition of the tides
the early morning tides are given ln the left
hand column and the successive tides of the
day ln the order of occurrence as to time. The
second time column gives the second tide of
the day. the third time column the third tide
and the last or right hand column gives the
last tide of the day, except when there are but
three tides, as sometimes occur. The heights
given are soundings to the soundings on the
United States Coast Survey charts, except
when a minus sign (â ) precedes the height,
and then the number given is subtracted from
the depth given by the charts. The plane of
reference Is the mean of tbe lower low waters.
