SERIOUS EIRE IN POTRERO.
An Early Morning Conflagration
Brings About the Loss of Two
Dwelling Houses.
An alarm was turned in at 1:15 o'clock
this morning from box 514 for a confla
gration on Michigan street, between
Twenty-first and Twenty-second streets,
Potrero. Two dwelling houses were de
stroyed and a second badly scorched. One
of them was owned by Mrs. Anne Judge
at 249% Michigan street. The house ad
joining her dwelling was unoccupied and
the owner is unknown.
The fire is said to have originated lit
the kitchen of Mrs. Judge's home. A pe
destrian homewardhound saw the smoke
emanating from the kitchen, and believ
ing it was a fire rushed to the branch
Potrero police station and the officer on
duty turned in the alarm. The fire was
speedily got under control by the de
partment. Mrs. Judge and her family
were in bed at the time, but managed to
get out without danger to their lives.
THE SAX FRANCISCO CALL,. SATURDAY, MAY 13, 1899.
GIGANTIC FRANCHISE STEAL
IS BEFORE THE SUPERVISORS
With Three Hundred Thousand Dollars
Huntington Seeks to Obtain a Six Per
Cent Interest on Two Millions.
.â â â -.' â  -
THE directing spirits of the Market Street Railroad Company have undertaken one of the
most gigantic schemes ever conceived in the interests : of C. P. Huntington and his asso
ciates. An effort is now quietly being made to monopolize street railroad privileges on one
of the great thoroughfares of the city, and to tap without competition for fifty years one of
the most populous districts of San Francisco. If the scheme succeeds the Market Street Rail
road Company will be able to water stock and issue bonds on a fictitious valuation of its property
and secure an enormous interest from, a comparatively small investment. If the plot meets the
sanction of the Board of Supervisors the Market Street Railroad Company will have won another
vicious victory and the charter government will be cheated out of the operation of one of its wisest
reforms.
Like all schemes of the railroad people, this new one is innocent enough on its face.
Among the many franchise privileges asked by the Market street company from the Board of
Supervisors ie one demanding the right to use electricity in the operation of a road, under an un
used franchise, on Geary street, from Grant avenue to Taylor street. In asking for this privi
lege the Market Street Railroad Company pleads that it is necessary in connection with the plan
to give more adequate street railway facilities to the northeastern part of the city, and to connect
that district with the Potrero. It is, therefore, from the point of view of the Market Street Rail
road Company, a matter of public interest to obtain the asked for privilege on Gear}' street.
The demand is nothing of the sort from a point of view which involves the public welfare.
In the first place, Geary street, between Grant avenue and Taylor street, is not necessary to con
nect the northwestern part of the city with the Potrero. There is a better and much more direct
route. In the second place, the unused franchise upon which the privilege of using electricity is
asked should now, under the law, be forfeited.
Under the plea of giving a public benefit the Market street company is planning to se
cure a tremendous monopoly. . If it can secure exclusive privileges on Geary street it will neces
sarily kill all competition and destroy the rest of the thoroughfare to all others for railroad pur
poses. But this is only the beginning of the great scheme.
The Geary street franchise, which the Market street company owns, will expire in 1902.
Under the charter, street railroad franchises may be granted for no longer period than twenty-five
years, and large contributions, based upon gross receipts, must be made to the city. The Market
Street Railroad Company wants to cheat the charter in reference to these restrictions, and will ask
the assistance of the Supervisors in doing so. Under the existing law a railroad franchise may be
surrendered at any time more than one year from its expiration.
The schemers of the Market Street Railroad Company intend, therefore, to surrender their
Gearv street franchise to the city and then secure a renewal. With this sharp play the Market
Street Railroad Company hopes to gain for its Geary street railway monopoly a new lease of life
in no way restricted by the operation of the charter. The treasury of the city will lose a great
revenue, legitimately its own, and the Market street company will be free- to water its stock at will
and issue bonds.
More than this, the Market Street Railroad Company will ask an extension of the Geary
street franchise to the ocean beach, making a continuous road from Kearny street out Geary and
Point Lobos avenue to the ocean. It is estimated that the construction of the road, if the franchise
be granted, will take no more than $300,000. The Market Street Railroad Company will then be
free to reap the enormous profit of its great steal. It will be exempt from the charter provisions,
which restrict the life of a franchise to twenty-five years. It will not have to pay the contributions
to the city as provided by the charter. These are 3 per cent of the gross receipts for the first five
years, 4 per cent of the gross receipts for the next ten years, and 5 per cent of the gross receipts
for the last ten years, with the forfeiture of the roadbed, track and stationary fixtures to the city.
It will readily be seen what tremendous profit will flow 7 to the Market street company if it
cheats the charter by this plot. Nor will this be all. The extension to the ocean beach, as already
indicated, will be made at a cost not exceeding $300,000. The Market street company will then
be able to water its stock at will, issue bonds, and with an expenditure of $300,000 receive in re
turn an interest of 6 per cent on $2,000,000. When the Market Street Railroad Company plans
a steal it schemes a big one. It remains with the Supervisors to determine with what success the
plot shall be rewarded.
TUGS RACING TO SECURE A TOW.
TRAMP STEAMER
ELIHU THOMSON
AT SANTA CRUZ
Tugs Racing to Secure
the Tow Here.
4,
The disabled steamer Elihu Thomson
anchored two miles off Lighthouse Point,
Santa Cruz, yesterday afternoon and will
probably be towed in through the Golden
Gate this morning. She was spoken by
the steamer Santa Rosa yesterday at 1:30
a. m. and Captain Alexander offered to
tow her Into port, but Captain Whitney
of tho Elihu Thomson refused, saying he
could make Santa Cruz or Monterey un
der sail. v '* .-:
As soon as the news- brought by the
Santa Rosa was made known the tug
Fearless -was at once sent out and an
hour later she was followed by the Res
cue. The two tugs when last seen -were
going full speed and using every ounce
of. steam, so it should be an interesting
race down the coast between them. The
Fearless ls a new vessel and -has never
been fully tried, but she can be depended
upon to give a good account of herself.
Tne Rescue Is a very fast tug, and If the
Fearless beats her down Captain Harry
Marshall will know he has been in a race.
The Elihu Thomson was .on her way
here from Honolulu with a cargo of su
gar. When several days -out- the shaft
broke and her propeller dropped to the
bottom of the ocean. . On April 30 the
transport Senator came along and sup
plied the disabled vessel with provisions,
besides taking off twelve firemen and oil
ers and one passenger. These were after
ward transferred to the steamer Austra
lia and brought to San Francisco. Later
the Thomson was spoken by the City of
Peking, and Captain Smith offered to tow
her into port, but Captain Whitney re
fused, saying he was making about .three'
knots an hour, under sail . and that he
would reach San Franclco all right.
As soon as the news reached here the
tug Relief was sent out to look for the
disabled steamer. She was followed by
the Rescue and later the underwriters
chartered the steamer Rival and sent her
out. The Rescue and Rival returned on
account of the fog and heavy weather,
but the Relief kept on and is out yet.
The underwriters then sent the Rival out
again and . Captain Perriman went along
to direct the course of the vessel and
to assist Captain Johnson. The Rival tis
still out and neither she nor the Relief
will return until their coal supply gives
In the meantime Captain Whitney kept
the Elihu Thomson dodging along under
canvas and has finally landed^ her at
Santa Cruz. There : is nearly $100,000 in
surance on the vessel and cargo, so by
refusing the offer of assistance made him
Captain Whitney has saved the under
writers a small fortune in salvage.
A dispatch from" Santa Cruz last night
stated that the Thomson would start for
this city at once in tow of a tug and would
arrive to-day. , â¢ â 
The Pacific -Mail Company steamers
San Juan and Acapuleo are a day be
hind time. The San Juan left Mazatlan
on' May 5 and the Acapuleo sailed from
China's Powerful Sword.
There Is . an ancient and dreadful eword ln
China. It gives to the man who happens to
hold It the power to cut off the head of any
one he wishes without danger of punishment
AH people flee from this sword as fearfully as
stomach Ills flee before the approach of Hos
tetter's Stomach Bitters. This famous remedy
cures all forms of stomach troubles/beginning
with constipation and ending with liver or
kidney disease. It will be found ln all drug
stores and It always cures.
Acapuleo on May 4. Both vessels were
expected here last Thursday, but the
chances are that they have been detained
by the heavy northwest weather that has
prevailed off the coast. The Acapuleo
comes from Panama with New York
freight and passengers, while the San
Juan halls from Acapuleo with coffee and
general merchandise.
The steamer Centennial, under charter
to the United States Government for a
transport, has been bought by Captain
Charles Nelson. She is a very handy ves
sel, and as soon as Uncle Sam is through
with her she will be put in the coast
trade. Captain Nelson now has three ves
sels under charter to the Governmentâ
the Cleveland, Charles Nelson and Cen
tennial. The first two are on their wav
here from Manila and the Centennial will
sail for the Philippines on Monday next.
The Occidental and Oriental Steamship ;

til the 25th inst. She was scheduled to '

Must Leave His Cottage.
John Hickey, who for twenty years or
more has occupied the picturesque little
cottage on the edge of the Buena Vista
Park Tract, has been notified by Superin
tendent McLaren that lie will be obliged
to move out at an early date, and that
the cozy house which has attracted the
attention of hundreds of passengers on
the Halght-street cars, must go down to
make room for improvements. Hickey
â¢has acted as a keeper for the park, and in
consideration of the valuable services
rendered In this way he has been allowed
to occupy the cottage rent free. The
house will be torn down to make room
for a new road.

POLICEMAN PHILLIPS
IS GLAD HE'S ALIVE
Police Officer Phillips Is congratulating
himself that he Is riot occupying a slab in
the Morgue.
Shortly after 3 o'clock yesterday morn
ing, while making his rounds, he discov
ered that the door of a cigar store owned
i.v W. Kraus at 70S Larkin street viae open.
Phillips entered the store, and after light
ing the gas went to the rear room, which
is occupied by Kraus and his wife. Mrs.
K rails heard him entering, and thinking
he was a burglar lustily shouted for the
police. Kraus was awakened by her
screams, and alsft concluding that tho
visitor was a desperate burglar he jumped
out of bed, and procuring his revolver
and a club started after the intruder.
By this time Phillip, had entered the
room and told the couple to keep quiet.
Evidently not recognizing the police offi
cer Kraus struck at Phillips with his
club. The officer Immediately grappled
with him and a struggle in the dark en
â sued. While the two men were struggling
for supremacy Mrs. Kraus recognized
Phillips and veiled to her husband to de
sist.
Kraus at once released his hold on the
officer and apologized for what he termed
his serious mistake. After cautioning the
merchant to keep his door locked in fu
ture Phillips left the store, well satisfied
with the fact that he was alive.

Joseph Ray was indicted by the Federal
Grand Jury yesterday for passing and
having In possession counterfeit half
dollars.

ALAMEDA COUNTY NEWS.
MAYOR SNOW
ON IMPROVEMENT
CLUBS' VALUE
Should Control the
Street Work.
â â
THE CITY'S ABOMINATION
â â
IT IS THE DISGRACEFUL CITY
HALL.
, pâ â
A Union of Clubs Looking to the Ad
vancement of Oakland Could
Do as Much as the Gov
ernment.
+
Oakland Office San Francisco Call,
SOS Broadway, May 12.
Mayer Snow is making the round of the
many improvement clubs and impressing
upon each the necessity of working to
gether. Mr. Snow takes the stand that j
the policy hitherto adopted by the city i
government of Oakland has been one of !
hit or miss, and consequently the city has I
been practically robbed of huge sums of
money for which no returns have been i
made.
In the Mayor's address to the improve- i
ment clubs he says: "The taxpayers own
a City Hall that is an abomination, and
for the sole reason that it has never re- j
ceived proper care. Its entrance is a
monument of official neglect. No man
mounts the steps of it entertaining respect
for the city government if he has brought
!any thus far. The whole structure, in- j
side and out, has taken on an indecent
appearance, and the city government is
justly juelged by it. With fifteen hundred |
dollars now and a few dollars from time
to time, all this could be changed. If it
were your private property it would be
changed without talking about it."
The Mayor has an idea that there should
be certain "centers" established through
cut the business portions of the city. One
man should be appointed tor each of these
centers, whose duty it would be to attend
to all i in- small pairs in the roads In his !
little district. Regarding this plan the j
Mayor says:
"Now the idea that I bring to you is to
the end that this be done. That it be
done by putting the right mar. in each of
these cent* giving him a hammer, saw,
nails, broom, shovel, hoe and a hand
cart; a box of broken stone will be kept,
convenient for him, and his work and dis
trict mapped out. There are various ordi
nances that have been passed from time
to time to prevent nuisances in the streets.
Of these there will be copies furnished for ;
distribution to property owners. I would I
not expect everything, but these men may
be made special policemen and so furnish
the protection by day that is not now
had with the whole police force. With
the right men in these central districts a
great deal of work will be accomplished,
and what Is just now equally desirable
after so great neglect, the public will be
witnesses of the Improved condition of
those affairs with which they come in
daily contact.
"A word as to the cost. The wages of
twelve men will aggregate $600 per month,
or $7000 per year. We spend three times
this sum now. but in such Intermittent,
hit or miss manner that the city govern
ment gets no credit for doing anything
nt all.
"1 desire to recommend to the City
Council the adoption of the plan I have
outlined, and if you and other Improve
ment clubs think well of it and will give
it your indorsement we can secure the
needed legislation after July 1 and make
the city shine."
Mayor Snow is a firm believer in the
usefulness of improvement clubs. In this
connection he says: r.v =
"I have a plan of organization that I
want to submit to you. The possible di
vision of the city Into patrol districts, as
already mentioned, has suggested the
plan to my mind. Naturally clubs are or
ganize about business centers. There are
about twelve Improvement clubs of im
portance In this city already organized.
The plan is to first disband the Central
Club of two hundred, so-called, that was
organized somo time ago In the center of
the city to further the park project. Then
of the chosen representatives of each of
the mprovement clubs a new Central
Club shall Ice formed. Clubs may have
two, four or ten representative, as may
be determined. This representative club
will not initiate measures, but every
measure of more than local Importance
arising in any. local club will be referred
to the club of representatives for its con
sideration and approval.
"The Council and the whole city govern
ment will be naturally and rightly guid
ed by the conclusions of the Central Club
of Representatives and sec will the whole
city. Of course, this club win have It in
mind to do. large things that affect the
whole city, and which will need, as we
have learned, the favor of the entire city
to carry to a successful conclusion."
LOOKS TO HEAVEN
FOR HER REWARD
Hopes, of a Woman Who Placards
Holy Writ on Uncle Collis'
Local Trains.
OAKLAND, May 12.â A perfect exam
ple of Christian acts performed on this
mundane globe for which a reward is
expected in heaven Is presented in the
liberal and cheerful manner In -which
Holy Writ Is quoted on large placards in
Uncle Collis' local trains for the edifica
tion of passengers who reside In the vir
tuous precincts of Alameda County.
In each car, alongside of placard- con
taining advertisements of shoes, cigars,
whisky and races, appears a card read
ing:
"Seek ye the Lord while he may be
found; call ye upon him while he is near.
Isaiah lv:6.
"For what Is a man profited If he shall
gain the whole world and lose his own
BOUl; or what shall a man give in ex
change for his soul. Matthew xvi:26.
"Repent ye, therefore, and be converted
thai your Fins may be blotted out. Acts
111:19.
Passengers were loth to believe that
these kindly reminders of Christian doc
trine were ordered posted In the cars by
Uncle Collis. To-day the first clew to the
party responsible for the cards was ob
tained. It is a woman residing in Oak
land, but her name could not be obtained.
This woman called on Morton L. Cook,
who operates and manages the car adver
tising Industry, some weeks ago, and
when told that the cost for the full sys
tem would be (70 per month, she stated
Flu- wished her cards placed only in cars
running through Alameda, Oakland and
Berkeley. This, the advertiser told her.
would cost but $50. The bargain was
struck, and the woman explained the
Scriptures she wished quoted.
"But, madam; there surely cannot be j
any monetary returns to you from such !
advertising." quoth the advertiser.
"I know," replied the Oakland Chris
tian. "There's no reward here for me. i
I will take your terms for three months, !

Preacher's Salary Attached.
OAKLAND, May 12.â Rev. V. Marshall
Law, rector of the Church of the Advent,
East Oakland, has special reasons,
through big sealed documentary evidence,
to know that plumbers' bills come high,
particularly when not paid promptly.
Plumber Smith a few days ago was given
Judgment for $2 90 and costs against the
rector. The costs amounted to $10 and
Justice Larue issued a writ attaching
Rev. Mr. Law's salary and to-day the
custodian of the church's strong box was
served with the papers, and now $12 90
â will be taken out of the rector's salary
for this month.
PRETTY GIRLS TO
PLAY BASKETBALL
Members of the Alameda High School
. Team at Practice*
ALAMEDA, May 12.â Alameda society promises to turn out to-morrow to
witness the basket-ball game between teams representing the Alameda
High School and the Longfellow School. The game will be played on
the lawn on the premises of Mrs. George H. Mastick, Pacific avenue,
near Chapin street. Ropes will be stretched around the beautiful grounds and
a detail of police will guard the outer lines.
The teams had their last practice to-day, and played with a skill that in
dicates that to-morrow's game will be closely contested and will prove a most
exciting exhibition of the sport which Alameda society has adopted as Its
latest fad.
The game Is given for the benefit of the Alameda Women's Charitable
Association, an organization composed of the most prominent ladles in the
enclnal city's social circles, whose practical work in charity deserves the
heartiest commendation. Many suffering families have been succored and
numerous worthy men upon whom Dame Fortune has frowned have been tided
over their difficulties by the ladles of the association. Through their assist
ance the unemployed have secured positions and the sick and needy have
been carefully provided for.
Since January 1 of this year the society has distributed to needy families
144 pieces of clothing, twenty-five pairs of shoes and thirty-four sacks of
coal. Aside from this, twenty visits have been made to the sick, employ
ment has been found for the heads of four families . and eleven different
families have been provided with the necessities of life.
It is to raise funds to continue this good work that the basket-ball game
is to be given. All Alameda will attend, and It Is calculated that the spa
â¢ cious Mastick grounds will be taxed to the limit to accommodate the crowd.
Among the prominent society ladles Interested in the association are the
following: Mrs. S. A. O'Nell, president; Mrs. M. F. McGurn, secretary; Mrs.
George H. Mastick, vice president; Mrs. R. W. McChesney, treasurer; Miss
Maude Jackson, collector; Mrs. George Palmer, Mrs. T. P. Tisdale, Mrs. A.
McCartney, Mrs. Gould, Mrs. Kane, Miss Mollie Kane, Mrs. C. S. Neal, Mrs.
G. N. Williams, Mrs. T. P. Winter, Mrs. McAnney, Mrs. George Plummer, Mrs.
Charles Holt, Mrs. C. L. Tilden. Mrs. Richard Kelly, Mrs. Peter White, Mrs.
H. Carpentier, Mrs. Bates. Mrs. O'Conner, Mrs, Mallen, Mrs. C. L. Mcl, Mrs.
Frank Otis, Miss Mary Mastick, Mrs. Peter Outcault, Mrs. Buckley, Mrs.
R. B. Mitchell, Miss Thorn, Mrs. C. J. O'Kell, Mrs. C. M. Smith, Mrs. Kruger,
Mrs. George Waggoner, Mrs. Joseph Leonard, Mrs. S. Scofield, Mrs. P. S.
Teller, Mrs. F. D. Colburn, Mrs. G. C. Bull. Mrs. Henry Hauch, Mrs. John
Ellsworth, Mrs. Dunkee, Mrs. Arthur Brown, Miss Edith Brown, Miss E.
Brown, Mrs. W. H. Souther, Mrs. E. Minor Smith, Mrs. Ed Anthony, Mrs.
John Mason, Mrs. John Metcalfe and Mrs. Allen Neal.

IN PERSIAN COSTUME
Class Day at the University of Cali
nia Will Be a Big, Spectacu
lar Affair.
BERKELEY, May 12.â T0-morrow will
be class day at the University of Cali
fornia. By tradition it is the day on which
the students whose four years' course is
complete celebrate the event by taking
their last farewell of the college before
joining the ranks of the alumni. The
class of 1899, which graduates this com
mencement, have made elaborate plans
for carrying out this old college custom.
Their celebration will consist of exercises
held both morning and afternoon.
At 9:30 o'clock in the morning the class,
headed by a band from the Presidio, will
start on its pilgrimage of farewell. From
the oaks on the lower campus the proces
sion will move to South Hall; where Earl
Swan is to make the address. From
there to all the college buildings in suc
cession the procession will move on, the
; order of buildings and the speaker at each

The afternoon exorcises are to be in the
nature of a spectacular Persian pageant,
held in the natural amphitheater behind
the chemistry building. All the members
of the class, both men and women, will
be arrayed ln Persian costumes. The
proceedings, Intended as an allegory of
college life, will be under the guise of a
Persian wedding. The seniors will there
recount the history of their four years'
life at college and one of them as a Per
sian astrologer will make the forecast of
their future. Among those taking the
more prominent parts are Earl Swan, Roy
V. Nye, Lloyd N. Scott and Charles E.
Thomas. The exercises will begin at 2
o'clock.

School Children Increase.
OAKLAND, May 12.â The school census
returns for Alameda County show an In
crease of about a thousand over last year.
The figures for the larger districts are:
Oakland, 15,993; Berkeley, 2955; Alameda,
3549; Laurel, 790; Fruitvale, 825; Llvermore,
045; Union, 7CT; San Lorenzo, 325.

GROSS ERRORS
IN A PRECINCT
AT PIEDMONT
Miller Gaining in the
Recount.
â  t â
MAY HAVE BEEN ELECTED
â * â
HAS OVERCOME HALF OF HIS
MINORITY.
Â« .
All the Patronage of the City Gov
ernment Depends Upon the Re
sult of the Contest, Which
Is Getting Closer.
Oakland Office San Francisco Call,
908 Broadway, May 12.
All the politicians, j office-holders and
placehunters became suddenly Interested
to-day in the Miller-Clement election con
test. During the progress of the recount
in the Second Ward a very grave errorâ
If not worseâ was discovered. In this one
precinct, which only contains 130 votes,
Mr. Miller gained twenty-nine. In the
Piedmont Precinct, which is. the sixteenth
in the Second Ward and one of the quiet
est and most respectable districts In the
city of Oakland, no one expected to find
any crookedness. In the Fourth and
Sixth wards Mr. Miller had alleged that
gross derelictions of duty had occurred,
but he was very modest in his claims of
miscounted votes in the conservative
Piedmont district. The discovery was like
a thunderclap In Judge Hail's courtroom.
Tlie election officers had tallied the votes
correctly and the tally sheets agreed with
the recount: "but on the return slip the
figures had been so changed that Instead
of leading by twenty-five votes Miller was
represented as being four behind. When
matters were straightened out Miller had
overcome at one clip three-eighths of his
entire minority. Another errorâ if it be
errorâ such as this will probably elect Mr.
Miller.
When the court closed last night there
were sixty-five ballots in the Sixth Ward
held out owing to the fact that they had
been numbered in lead pencil. This morn
ing the election officers proved to the sat
isfaction of the court that these marks
had been put on by the board during the
counting, and the ballots were admitted.
The election officers of the Piedmont
district who are responsible for the pecu
liarity of the returns are: Inspectors W.
T. Harris and E. R. Hill, Judges W.
O'Connor and J. H. Mochler, Clerks W.
Blair and S. J. Agnew, Ballot Clerks W.
B. Sanford and C. A. Surphlis.
They may be subpenaed to appear be
fore the court and explain the wide dis-
crepancy.
Should Mr. Miller be elected there will
be more excitement than over the whole
of the recent city election, for with Mr.
Dow he would at .once proceed upon such
a wholesale cleaning-out process that
there would hardly be anybody left at the
City Hall to tell the tale of what had
been. All through the campaign the cry
was "Dow and Miller." conveying the; Im
pression that Mayor Snow would be more
conservative and less likely to make a
combination than the other two Republi
cans. When Miller was defeated there
was big disappointment, and since the
new government took office and the
Mayor has shown no desire to turn things
upside down there has been much disap
pointment among the politicians. The
progress of the Miller-Clement contest ls
being hourly watched with great anxiety.
When the recount closed to-night Miller
had gained 41 votes out of 80. There are
only the Seventh and Fifth wards to be
counted. About 200 ballots are held ln re
serve, but they are about equally dis
tributed. ;
POSITION OF CABMEN.
Not Organized to Oppose the Plans of
Employers.
OAKLAND, May 12.â David Hughes,
the official reporter of the Carmen's So
cial and Benevolent Society, has written
to The Call stating that one paragraph
in a recent article on the hardships of
carmen does not agree with the facts,
i "The article In The Call," says Mr.
D. W. Landers, John Whiting. J. F.
Gassmann, E. B. Smith, M. Elssler, A.
C. Cooper, F. Rouxand, J. Raphael,
both sides being satisfied to go on
with the eleven. "Little Egypt" sat
. beside her attorney and was apparent
ly an object of curiosity to the jurors.
It was expected that she would be
asked to testify, but the crowd in the
court room was doomed to disappoint
ment.
The only witness examined was Po
liceman Esola, who testified to what
he saw the defendant do prior to plac
ing her under arrest. He was asked
to give an Imitation of the dance, but
blushlngly begged to be excused. It
was admitted. by the defendant's at
torney that the evidence of Policeman
Tyrrell would be the same as that of
Esola, and the prosecution rested. At
torney Dunne said he had no evidence
to offer, and the case was submitted
to the jury without argument. ;
The Jury retired, and after being out
about twenty minutes returned with a
verdict of acquittal. The peculiarity
of the verdict is that it is directly op
posed to the decision of Judge Conlan.
The Judge heard the case against the
woman the second time she was ar
rested and It was not nearly so strongs
as the case before the Jury, but the
Judge convicted her and sentenced
her to pay a fine of $100. That decision
was appealed from and is still pending
In the Superior Court. The cases of
the other dancers arrested with "Lit
tle Egypt" i are yet to be tried."
Hughes, "is correct as to the facts relat
ing to our having to put up a deposit and
to carry insurance. The last paragraph
is incorrect. It is true that there is an
organization of the carmen employed on
the various roads in Oakland, but they
are not organized for any antagonistic
purposes toward the people or their em
ployers. It is a beneficial society, pure
and simple, caring for its sick members
as would any other beneficial order. Poli
tics, religion or any business not connect
ed with the society is not tolerated for
a moment, and no one is authorized to
use the name of the society in that con
nection.
"Certain unscrupulous persons have en
deavored to- injure us In the community
and with our employers, but we have ab
solutely no desire to oppose the methods
of our employers, although we may not
all agree that they are just the right
thing. We wish to be set right on this
point."

ANNUAL Meetingâ The regular annual meeting
of the stockholders of the Olympic Salt Water
Company will be held at 327 Market st, San
Francisco, Cal., on MONDAY, the 22d day of
May, 1899, at the hour of 3 p. m., for the pur-
pose of electing a Board of Directors to serve
for the ensuing year, and the transaction of
such other business as may come before the
meeting. Transfer books will close on Fri-
day, May 12, 1899, at 3 o'clock p. m.
CHAS. A. GIBSON, Secretary.
Office. 32? Market St., San Francisco, Cal.
NOTICE is hereby given by order of the Board
of Directors of the OCEANIC STEAMSHIP
COMPANY that a meeting of the stockholders
of said Company has been called by said
Board, to be held on FRIDAY, the second day
of June, A. D. 1599, at 11 o'clock in the fore-
noon of said day, at the principal place of
business of said Company, at the building
where the said Board of Directors usually
meets, namely, at the oflfce of said Company,
number 327 Market st., in the City and County
of San Francisco, State of California; that
the object of said meeting Is to consider and
act upon the proposition that said Company
create a bonded indebtedness of two million
five hundred thousand dollars ($2,500,000), ln
United States gold coin, for the purpose of
raising money to complete the construction
of its steamships and their equipment, for
use In the business of this corporation, and
to purchase and pay for any other property
within the purposes of this Company; and to
secure the bonded Indebtedness so proposed
to be created by a mortgage upon Its steam
and sailing ships and all other property of
said Company now owned or hereafter to be
acquired by said Company.
By order of the Board of Directors of the
Oceanic Steamship Company.
[Corporate Seal.] E. H. SHELDON
Secretary of the Oceanic Steamship Com-
pany. ;

NOTICE.
To All Whom It May Concernâ Notice ls
hereby given that on the 17th day of April,
1899, the Bakersfield and Los Angeles Rail-
way Company filed Its application in writing,
ln the office of the Clerk of the Superior
Court of the City and County of San Fran-
cisco, addressed to the Superior Court of said
county, making application for the voluntary
dissolution of the Bakersfield and Los An-
geles Railway Company, which said petition
and application stated that at a meeting of
the stockholders of said company on the 13th
? day of April, 1899, called for the purpose of
passing upon the question. It was resolved to
have a voluntary dissolution of the corpora-
tion, and that such resolution was passed by
a two-thirds vote of all the stockholders, and
that all the claims and demands against the
corporation, of every kind, nature and de-
scription, have been fully paid and dis-
charged.
Wherefore, It was ordered by the said Su-
perior Court upon the filing of said petition
and application that the undersigned, as
Clerk of said Court, give a notice of such ap-
plication by publication In some newspaper
published <In the City and County of San
Francisco, for a period of thirty-five days. .~
Therefore, all persons having any Interest-
In the matter of said application or desiring
to oppose the granting of the same are here-,
â¢ by notified to file In my office, on or before
the 22d day of May. 1899, , any and all objec-
tions which they may have to the granting of
said petition. â¢ ' -
In 'testimony whereof, I have | hereunto set
my hand and the official seal of said court
this the 17th day of April, 1899.
(Seal), WILLIAM A. DEANE,
County Clerk and ex-Offlcio Clerk of the
Superior Court.
By E. M. THOMPSON.
Deputy County Clerk and ex-Officio Deputy
Clerk of the Superior Court

COURT CLERK KELLY
AND J.N. E.WILSON
TO BE INDICTED
Held Responsible for the Re
lease of the "Strangler."
Investigation Reveals That Thomas Cos
grove, One of the Supposed Bonds
men, Is a Myth.
CLERK KELLY of Judge Graham's
court and Attorney J. N. E. Wilson
will be indicted by the Grand Jury
for their participation in the at
tempted escape of Harry "Wilson,
who was recently arrested on several
charges of petty larceny.
The colored man was taken Into cus
tody several weeks ago on suspicion of
being the strangler of May McDermott
end Bertha Para creatures of the
tenderloin, who were strangled to death,
presumably for the purpose of robbery.
Failing to connect Wilson with the hor- <
J. N. E. WILSON.
rlble crimes. Chief Lees, after ascertain- ,
Ing that he had robbed several women by
representing himself as a wealthty cattle
dealer of Montana, ordered three charges
of petty larceny to be placed against him.
He was convicted on each charge, and
committed to jail for eighteen months,
the maximum penalty.
The prisoner's attorney was J. N. E.
Wilson, who, the police say, was impli
cated in the escape of Gilbert Kendall, a
desperate counterfeiter. Kendall for some
unknown reason was taken from the City
Prison to Wilson's office, and during the
temporary obsence of a Deputy Sheriff,
who accompanied him, he jumped
through a window and made his escape.
THOMAS KELLY.
Wilson, the colored man, was taken be
fore the Grand Jury yesterday and read
ily consented to toll how he secured his
release on straw bonds.
"Attorney Wilson visited me in the City
Prison," he declared, "and after inform
ing me that I had been booked on three
charges of petty larceny, offered to de
fend me. I told him I was penniless, but
promised to reward him for his services
'as soon as I was set at liberty.'
"Immediately after I was convicted and
sentenced to eighteen months' Imprison
ment Wilson again .'islted me In my cell
ln the City Prison and asked me If I could
-**â¢Â« thrfe hundred dollars. He promised
Ito secure my release as soon fts that
1 amount was handed to him. I informed
i him that I had a friend, a woman, who
would gladly give that sum if he succeed
ed in having me released from jail. I as
sured him that if he arranged It so that
! I could get out, even for a short time,
I would take him to my friend's room
and personally see that he was given the
amount he asked for. Evidently realizing
that I meant what I said Wilson left me
after promising to return and take me
out on bonds. A few hours later he re
turned to the prison with the necessary
bonds. I was then taken out of my cell
and told that I was at liberty to go.
"As I left the prison Wilson followed
! me and asked if I Intended to keep my
promise as to the three hundred dollars he
was to receive for his services in securing
my release. â¢ I answered in the affirma
tive and together we went to my friend's
room on Fourth street, near Howard.
, She was surprised to see me, but after I
. had explained how I had been released on
bonds through the efforts of the attorney
she seemed satisfied.
"I informed her of my promise to pay
Wilson $300 and she at once went to her
trunk and. taking out three $100 bills,
handed them to him. He promptly ac
cented the money and shortly afterward
left the place.
"As he assured me that I was at lib
erty to go where. I pleased, I took the
j first train for Santa Cruz, intending to
i remain there a few days and then go to
Mex'co. Thinking that Wilson had
squared the cases against me as he rep
: resented, I was disagreeably surprised a
few days'later when a Constable arrested
! me on a dispatch from Chief Lees."
The colored prisoner tthen told how
! after he had been brought back to this
; city he was confronted with Wilson, the
attorney, in the presence of Captain Sey
mour and accused him of having thrown
j him down, as he expressed It.
"Did you give me any money to defend
you?" excitedly asked the attorney at the
! time. -
"Yes, I did; you got $300 from me and
then gave me the worst of It," retaliated
j the prisoner. v

fore the Grand Jury, and furthermore de
clared that the attorney begged him not
to tell the police that he had received a

It was developed at the investigation
that the Thomas Cosgrove, who was sup
nosed to have qualified as one of the
bondsmen on Wilson's bond, is a myth.
As ex-Deputy Sheriff Riley, whose name
also appears on the bond, denies that It
is his signature, the police are morally
certain that somebody is guilty of forg
ery.
At the conclusion of the investigation
yesterday several of the grand jurors
were iin favor of indicting both Kelly
and Attorney Wilson. The , matter was
discussed at length, but it was finally de
cided to postpone action until next Mon
day. It is authoritatively said that at
the next meeting of the inquisitorial body
true bills will be found against the two
men.
Kelly does not bear a very good repu
tation. Some time ago he was arrested
by Detective Egan on suspicion of being
a pickpocket, but as the charge could not
be sustained he was released.
FRIEDLANDER AND
BELASCO MIX THINGS
THEATRICAL MANAGERS USE
ANGRY WORDS.
An Incident Not Down on the Bills
Furnishes Excitement for a
Deadhead Audience on
O'Farrell Street.
S. H. Friedlander. late of the firm of
Friedlander, Gottlob & Marx, and Fred
Belasco of the Alcazar furnished an ex
citing performance last night not down on
the bills. The audience, all deadheads,
was composed of people who happened to
be in the vicinity of that part of O'Far
rell street most brilliantly lighted by
theatrical stars.
Friedlander made his entrance P. D. Q.
from the corridor of the Alcazar to the
street, closely followed by Belasco, in the
role of the heavy villlan bent on r-r-r-re
venge. There was no mistaking the situa
tion. A wild and woolly melodrama could
not have been more realistic. Everybody
saw in an instant that- Belasco was a bad
Indian after the scalp of the pale-faced
fugitive. It looked to be all up with
Friedlander and the audience began to
hold its breath for the climax, when along
came a westbound car, just like the to-be
continued-in-our-next line in the "Boys'
and Girls' Own," to foil the villain. Fried
lander was saved. The last seen of him
he was loping up Powell street, far from
Belasco and the "Heart of the Tender
loin."
Exciting as was this scene, the previous
encounter between the theatrical mag
nates was more so. In It Friedlander is
said to have received a slap In the face
from Belasco and to have been aided in
his subsequent entrance on the street
scene by the muscular action of the Al
cazar n:antger. Up to the time of their
collision the two men had been good
friends. Belasco blames Friedlander for
having caused the breach. It seems that
Belasco & Thall have on the road a dra
matic company in which there are two
young men whom Friedlander was desir
ous of securing for some attraction in
which he was interested. With this end
in view he wrote the actors, offering them
an engagement. They, being novices at
the business. immediately forwarded the
letter to Belasco. Not hearing from the
young men. Friedlander, after a week or
so. wrote another letter, receiving in due
course a reply informing him that his
first letter had been forwarded to Belasco
and referring him to that gentleman for
information. Last night, according to all
accounts, Friedlander called on Belasco
for the purpose of assuring him that he
meant nothing wrong in writing as he
had. The act began and ended as stated.
BASEBALL TO-DAY.
Oakland and "Watsonville Teams to
Contest.
Watsonville will invade the local base
ball preserves to-day, and Manager Cal.
Ewing and his bunch of rejuvenated crip
ples have been busy all the week getting
themselves in readiness to give the sugar
growers a fitting welcome. The reception
will take place at Recreation Park, begin
ning at 3 o'clock, and a large time may be
confidently expected by those who are in
attendance. The Watsonvilles, . flushed
with their string of victories, declare that
nothing can stop them now, not even
Uncle Hank's constellation of sluggers,
and that nothing on the coast ever
equaled them in hitting and fielding.
During the week Oakland has made
some changes in its make-up that will
prove most beneficial and stops the only
leak in an otherwise water-tight Infield.
"Kid" Peeples. one of the best infielders
in the State, will play second and Dun
leavy will cover first, Shea, who is a good
outfielder and hard hitter but an indif
ferent pitcher, going into left garden. The
teams are practically tied for fourth posi
tion now, are evenly matched, and this
week's series between them will doubtless
be as close and exciting as any games I
ever played here. The line-up of the I
teams to-day is as follows: j

The final game of the Academic Ath
letic League baseball tournament for this
year's championship will be played at
Recreation Park this morning at 10 o'clock
sharp. The championship lies' between
Palo Alto and Stockton high schools, which
have qualified for the final. Both teams
have exceedingly good records and are
capable of putting up a fast and interest
ing game. W. W. Smith, who last year
was captain of Hoitt's team and of the
University of California, will umpire.

ATTORNEY ACH THREATENS.
Informs Officers Esola and Tyrrell
That Their Admittance to the
Cafe Royal Will Be Barred.
Attorney Ach is determined that the
police must not bother his clients, Messrs.
Hornlein & Co., proprietors of the Cafe
Royal. He visited the place last even
ing and finding Officers Tyrrell and Esola
on guard he tersely Informed them that
if they tried to watch the tables to-night
they would have to break in with an ax.
He Insinuated that admission to the po
licemen would be barred.
On their return to the Southern station
the. officers reported what Mr. Ach had
said to Captain Spillane, and the latter
became indignant. He said he would send
both men to the gambling resort again
to-night and he did not think they would
find any difficulty in getting inside. Cap
tain Spillane was also informed that three
tables were occupied by card players dur
ing the evening, and although money was
not handled the percentage was used ln
buying rounds of drinks and packs of
cards. It is expected that there will be a
lively time in the cafe to-night.
SAM YUP BENEFIT.
The Jackson-Street Theater Crowded
With Chinese and Hundreds
Turned Away.
A special entertainment was given In |
the Jackson street theater last night by
the Sam Yups and it was so largely pat
ronized that it was found "necessary to
call for the assistance of the police to
keep back the crowds.
The theater will hold about 1500 white
men or about 2000 Chinese. The thrifty
Sam Yups managed to dispose of about
4000 tickets, and as a result at about 9
o'clock a notice was posted at the doors
that the house was filled and no more
people would be admitted. The Chinese
who held tickets kept crowding up to the
doors all night, and many of them, when
informed that they could not be admitted
made remarks which If translated would
be unfit for publication. They all held
tickets and naturally thought they were
victims of a bunko game.
The proceeds will be devoted to the Sam
Yup joss house on Waverly place.

TO SAVE THE STATES
WATERS AND FORESTS
A Big Convention of Interested
Organizations to Be Held.
California Water and Forest Society Takes the Initia
tive in the Matter and Will Ask Co-operation
of Kindred Bodies.
AN executive meeting of the members
of several committees of the Cali
fornia Water and Forest Society
was held at the Merchants' Club
yesterday afternoon and steps were taken
for the holding of a big convention in
this city with the object of conserving
the waters of the State.
United States' Surveyor General J. M.
Gleaves, president of the organization,
was chairman, and there were present
Colonel J. P. Irish of the executive com
mittee; Professor George Davidson, chair
j man of the committee on flood waters and
I reservoirs; W. H. Mills, chairman of the
i committee on printing and publication;
Hugh Craig of the committee on organi
zation; ex-Mayor Pond; F. W. Dohrmann,
president of the Merchants' Association;
William Thomas, M. Hopkins and Mayor
! Phelan, who had been invited to attend
I the conference.
A general discussion was had as to
I the surest means of getting the sentiment
i of the people at large in regard to the
I best plan to utilize the waters of the
I State, when and where needed, In order
i to bring about the conservation of the
( watersheds and forests. Some practical
i suggestions were offered and after the
subject had been thoroughly gone Into
I President Gleaves appointed a committee
j of five to meet with representatives of
i the commercial, mining, farming and oth
j er interested bodies in the State and ar
| range for a State convention to be held
I in this city. â  <;'â¢" \^
The purpose of this convention will be
, to induce the State to appropriate suffi-
I cient funds for the construction of im
! pounding reservoirs. Should the State fall
] to pass needed legislation then the con
vention will devise ways and means to
j bear the expense. The committee will
I consist of Colonel John P. Irish, Elliott
; McAllister. Julian Sonntag, Edward. F.
Adams, Cralgle Sharp and J. M. Gleaves
! (ex-offlclo), all members of the executive
, committee. The meeting will . not take I
THE NEW SUPREME
COURT COMMISSIONER
THE Supreme Court Justices yesterday appointed James A. Cooper as Supreme
Court Commissioner, vice E. J. Pringle, deceased. The new Commissioner
is a member of the law firm of Cooper & Knight, who have offices in the
Mills building, â¢'
Mr. Cooper has been a resident of this city only for the past nine months, his
former home being in Ukiah, Mendocino County, where for twenty-one years he
practiced law. He comes from an old and well known Southern family of North
Carolina. Leaving the South at an early age he went to Kansas, but the future
of that State did not appeal to him. and In 1576 he came to this State, settling
at Uklah. For a long time he read Jaw in the office, of Judge Thomas of Santa
Rosa, Judge Thomas being then a law partner of Judge (now Justice) Temple.
Judge Temple appointed the young lawyer to the position of Court Commissioner
of the old District Court, the only public office Mr. Cooper ever held.
Since then he has been offered several positions, but his practice was too val
uable to be abandoned. He was associated with H. E. Highton in the celebrated
"King" White divorce case, appearing for the wife. Soon after the election of Gov
ernor Budd the position of Code Commissioner was offered to Mr. Cooper, but he de
clined with thanks. Shortly before he came to this city Mr. Cooper was one of
the directors of the Bank of Ukiah. He has been a delegate several times to the
Democratic State Convention. Since his residence in this city Mr. Cooper has
made his home at the Hotel Pleasanton with his family, consisting of a wife,
son and daughter. The son is now at Harvard College. During the summer the
family resides at Sausallto.
The Supreme Court Justices have renewed the appointments of the former
Court Commissioners, which. now consist of John Haynes, N. P. Chipman,
Wheaton A. Gray, E. W. Britt and J. A. Cooper. The secretary of the commis
sion was not named.

President Gleaves announced the ap
pointment of Hugh Craig, former presi
dent of the Chamber of Commerce, as a
member of the executive committee ot
the society to succeed William C. Ralston,
who has resigned on account of pressure
of other business.
Mr. Gleaves presented a history of the
Water and Forest Society from its incep
tion down through the successive stages
of legislation, and bespoke for it great
success. When seen in regard to the pro
posed convention, he said:
."The water and forestry question is of
momentous import to California's best in
terests, and this convention will be an Im
portant one for that reason, as it will
deal in a practicable manner with the
problem which confronts us. The whole
subject at the present time is a matter
for investigation solely. The plan for
bonding the State for the purposes of
water conservation will be thoroughly
considered in all its phases, and much
good will result therefrom. . It Is but just
that posterity should assist in paying for
the benefits that will certainly accrue by
a proper solution of the problem. The
last Legislature passed a bill appropriat
ing $10,000 for the work, but It failed to
get through in time. By the holding of a
convention the necessity of State aid will
become apparent.
"The various committees of the society
report progress in its affairs. Chairman
J. M. Walling of the executive commit
tee, Julian Sonntag of the finance com
mittee, Edward F. Adams of the com
mittee on organization, John P. Irish of
.the committee on forestry, Professor
George Davidson of the flood waters and
reservoir committee. E. W. Hilgard of
the committee on distribution of waters
and Curtis H. IJndley of the committee
on legislation .have all been performing
yeoman service in the good cause, and re
port a growing interest In the objects for
which the society was organized."

POKER PLAYERS
RAVE CAUSE OR
JUBILATION
Case Against Hugo A.
Hornlein Tried.
â * â
ELEVEN JURORS ACQUITTED
â â¦ â
ONLY ONE MAN STOOD OUT FOB,
CONVICTING DEFENDANT.
Â» . â
Stress Laid Upon the Fact That the
Players Are Provided With Re
freshments for Checks
* Taken.
â¦
The trial of H. A. Hornlein, proprietor
of the Cafe Royal, before a. jury in Judge
Mogan's court yesterday on the charge of
permitting a percentage game of poker
j to be played in his place, resulted in a
I disagreement, eleven being for acquittal
[ and one for conviction. -j -.": v;
The evidence was noteworthy in the
fact that Policeman Hemenez made a
very poor witness, and had to be con
stantly led by either Prosecuting Attor
ney Joachimsen or the Judge, against At
torney Ach's constant objections; . and
also in Sergeant M. J. Conboy's definition
of the word "percentage," which was
startling.
Hemenez testified to having entered the
cafe on the night of April 24. On going
to the rooms reserved for poker he saw
five men playing with chips. A man
named Berry, or Barry, was standing be
hind one of the players, and when each
player put in his ante Barry took out a
chip as a "rake off." Hemenez admitted
not being familiar with a percentage
game. After seeing two games played he
notified Sergeant Conboy and the sergeant
and Officers Heffernan, Donovan and
Murphy came into the poker room and
stood looking on while two other games
were played. Then the players were ar
â¢rested. In cross-examination Hemenez
said he did not see a card reading. "Un
lawful Games Prohibitedâ Minors Not
Allowed," nor did he see a card contain
ing the rules of the game.
. Sergeant Conboy corroborated Hemen
cz's testimony, and said positively that a
percentage game was being played. On
cross-examination he said he had read
law and knew the meaning of percent
age. It meant a commission or allow
ance, and on being further pressed he
said It meant a commission for services
rendered or a commission in the army.
Ach pressed him. so hard on that point
that he lost his temper and warned Ach
not to try and make a fool of him. He
admitted he had never heard the game
called a percentage game until the ar
rests were made. He saw no cigars,
drinks or sandwiches given to the play
ers.
The defendant took the stand and tes
tified that the chips taken by the con
ductor of the game were for drinks, ci
gars or sandwiches to the players, and
for the use of the room and cards. He
had no intention of violating the law,
and before he allowed poker to be played
in the rooms three years ago he asked
permission from Captain Spillane. and
the captain had* told him it would be
all right. Captain Spillane was sent
for, and he frankly admitted that he had
j told Hornlein it would be all right, but at
that time he did not know that poker
playing for a percentage was illegal.
This ended the testimony, and after the
addresses of counsel and the Judges
charge the jury retired, and after being
out for about two hours they announced
that they could not agree. They stood
eleven for acquittal and one for convic
tion. â¢. -. â  â  . _, â¢ .
The case was continued until Monday
to be again set for trial along with the
other cases.
Two Small Fires.
An alarm was turned in yesterday after
noon at 4 o'clock for a fire in the three
story frame building 413-415 Golden Gate
avenue, owned by D. L. Farnsworth. and
ocupied by S. Shatshery as a dwelling
house. The fire was caused by sparks
from a chimney, and the loss was > not
great. â¢â â¢ â  â¢ _ .
At 5:45 an alarm was turned In from
box 68. The fire was in the three-story
building at 22 Bluxome street owned by
I A. Hill and occupied by S. Peterson as a
dwelling. Cause, sparks from a chimney:
loss nominal.
FAINTED AT HIS POST.
ALAMEDA, May 12.â The first plans ot
the new Board of City Trustees toward
economizing in the management of the
electric light works have miscarried. The
force was reduced, and those who held
their positions were compelled to do duty
for upward of fourteen hours a day. This
afternoon J. O. -lesser, the, engineer,
fainted at his post. When he was able he
notified the City Trustees that he could
not stand the strain and would resign his
position. Under the new arrangement he
was forced to do the wiper's work in ad
dition to his duties as engineer. Had he
fainted during the night shift, when his
engine was running, the valuable ma
chinery of the electric light plant might
have been ruined before his condition was
discovered. To-night the plant is being
run by A. V. Fisher, who was formerly
wiper, but who was promoted to the posi
tion of collector and bookkeeper.
